30 +- **Rate limiting:** a global limiter of 200 requests / 15 minutes / IP applies to all `/api` routes.
          +This is the single most important constraint for load testing EShop — at 50 or 500 virtual users, the
          +limiter would return HTTP 429 after roughly 200 requests, invalidating any SLO measurement, unless byp
          +assed. The backend supports a `LOADTEST=1` startup flag specifically to disable this limiter for measu
          +rement runs.
      131 +
      132 +### 5.3 Testing Scope
      133 +
      134 +The performance test targets the following EShop flows, matching the workload model required by the se
          +minar brief:
      135 +
      136 +- **Browse** — `GET /api/products` (product listing).
      137 +- **Search** — `GET /api/products?search=<keyword>` (parameterized `LIKE` query; note the real search
          +endpoint is `/api/products?search=`, not `/api/products/search`, which instead falls through to the `:
          +id` route and returns an empty `200` response).
      138 +- **Add to Cart** — `POST /api/cart`, requires `Authorization: Bearer <token>`.
      139 +- **Checkout** — `POST /api/checkout`, requires `Authorization: Bearer <token>`; the endpoint trusts t
          +he client-submitted `total_amount` without server-side recalculation.
      140 +
      141 +Out of scope: admin-only endpoints, non-HTTP protocols, and any UI-level (browser) testing — this is a
          +n API-level performance test.
      142 +
      143 +### 5.4 Test Environment
      144 +
      145 +[TODO: document the actual machine(s) used for baseline/spike execution — OS, CPU, RAM, Node.js versio
          +n, whether backend and load generator run on the same host or separate hosts, and network conditions.
          +This must be filled in before the SLO report in Section 15 can be interpreted correctly, since local s
          +ingle-machine execution affects both the load generator's own resource contention and the results.]
      146 +
      147 +### 5.5 Limitations of the Environment (Node.js + SQLite + Local)
      148 +
      149 +- SQLite serializes writes through a single connection; under concurrent checkout/cart writes, content
          +ion at the database layer is expected to be the primary bottleneck rather than the HTTP layer — this i
          +s a hypothesis to be confirmed in Section 15, not yet a measured result.
      150 +- Running the load generator (k6/JMeter) on the same machine as the backend under test can introduce r
          +esource contention that skews latency measurements upward; this is a known threat to validity, tracked
          + in Section 18.
      151 +- The environment is local and single-instance; it does not represent a production deployment with loa
          +d balancing, connection pooling, or a multi-core/clustered Node.js process.
      152 +
      153  ---
      154
      155  ## 6. Tool Survey Methodology
       43 -- Evaluation criteria
       44 -- Survey methodology
       45 -- Tool selection process
      156
      157 +### 6.1 Evaluation Criteria
      158 +
      159 +Following `docs/S1_Tool_Survey_Proposal.md`, candidate tools are compared against five criteria:
      160 +
      161 +1. **Learning curve** — how quickly the team can become productive with the tool's scripting model.
      162 +2. **Protocol / HTTP support** — coverage of HTTP methods, headers, authentication, and correlation ne
          +eded to script EShop's login → browse → cart → checkout flow.
      163 +3. **CI/CD integration** — how well the tool runs headless/non-interactively, suitable for scripted, r
          +epeatable execution.
      164 +4. **Reporting / visualization** — quality of built-in metrics reporting (percentiles, error rate) wit
          +hout needing extra tooling.
      165 +5. **Scalability** — ability to generate the required load (50 VU baseline, up to 500 VU spike) from t
          +he available hardware.
      166 +
      167 +### 6.2 Survey Methodology
      168 +
      169 +The team identified five open-source candidate tools with an established community and active document
          +ation: k6, Apache JMeter, Locust, Gatling, and Artillery. Each tool was scored against the five criter
          +ia above using official documentation as the primary source. Where the team used an LLM to help draft
          +the initial comparison (per the course's suggested prompt template for tool-comparison matrices), ever
          +y claim was cross-checked against official documentation before being included in this report, consist
          +ent with the course's AI usage policy (Section 20).
      170 +
      171 +### 6.3 Tool Selection Process
      172 +
      173 +The five candidates were narrowed to a shortlist of two — one primary and one secondary tool — based o
          +n fit with EShop's technology stack (Node.js) and the team's existing JavaScript skill set, plus the s
          +eminar's explicit requirement to implement the same workload model in **both** a scripting-first tool
          +and a GUI-capable tool. The resulting selection and rationale are detailed in Section 8.
      174 +
      175  ---
      176
      177  ## 7. Performance Testing Tool Survey
       50 -- Survey of candidate tools
       51 -- Feature comparison
       52 -- Strengths and weaknesses
      178
      179 +### 7.1 Survey of Candidate Tools
      180 +
      181 +The following general characteristics are drawn from each tool's official documentation and are not sp
          +ecific to EShop; they establish the baseline for the comparison in Section 7.2. [TODO: the team should
          + verify version numbers and add citations to Section 21 (References) before final submission.]
      182 +
      183 +- **k6** — open-source load testing tool written in Go, with test scripts authored in JavaScript. CLI-
          +first (no built-in GUI), designed for scripted and CI/CD-friendly execution. Provides built-in thresho
          +ld and check APIs for pass/fail SLO assertions, and a text/JSON summary output; richer dashboards are
          +available through the optional k6 Cloud service or third-party exporters (e.g., to Grafana).
      184 +- **Apache JMeter** — mature, Java-based load testing tool (originally released in the late 1990s) wit
          +h both a GUI (for authoring `.jmx` test plans) and a headless/CLI mode (`-n -t ... -l ... -e -o ...`)
          +recommended for actual load generation. Supports a wide range of protocols beyond HTTP (JDBC, JMS, FTP
          +, SOAP) and has a large plugin ecosystem. Generates HTML dashboard reports from CLI runs.
      185 +- **Locust** — Python-based load testing tool where user behavior is defined as Python code rather tha
          +n a DSL or GUI. Supports distributed, master/worker execution to scale beyond a single machine, and pr
          +ovides a web UI for monitoring a running test.
      186 +- **Gatling** — high-performance load testing tool with an async, non-blocking execution engine (Scala
          +-based core, with newer Java/JavaScript SDK options). Produces detailed HTML reports and integrates wi
          +th build tools (Maven/Gradle/sbt) for CI. The open-source edition is CLI-only; a paid Enterprise editi
          +on adds a web-based control plane.
      187 +- **Artillery** — Node.js-based load testing tool using YAML or JavaScript test definitions. Lightweig
          +ht and CLI-first, a natural fit for teams already using the Node.js/npm toolchain; a paid cloud add-on
          + offers distributed/serverless load generation.
      188 +
      189 +### 7.2 Feature Comparison
      190 +
      191 +| Tool | Learning Curve | Protocol/HTTP Support | CI/CD Integration | Reporting/Visualization | Scalab
          +ility | Notes |
      192 +|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
      193 +| k6 | Low–medium for JS-familiar teams | Strong HTTP/1.1 & HTTP/2; WebSocket, gRPC support | Strong —
          + CLI-first, Docker-friendly | Built-in summary + thresholds; richer dashboards via k6 Cloud/Grafana |
          +Good on a single machine; distributed/cloud execution needs k6 Cloud or k6 Operator | Best alignment w
          +ith EShop's Node.js/JS stack |
      194 +| Apache JMeter | Medium — GUI eases entry, `.jmx` XML is verbose | Very broad (HTTP, JDBC, JMS, FTP,
          +SOAP, etc.) | Good in headless (`-n`) mode; GUI mode is **not** suitable for real load | Built-in HTML
          + dashboard report (`-e -o`) | Higher per-VU memory/CPU overhead due to JVM + thread-per-VU model | Mat
          +ure, large plugin ecosystem |
      195 +| Locust | Medium — requires comfort writing Python classes | Good HTTP support; other protocols need
          +custom clients | Good — headless mode available | Built-in web UI is real-time but less report-file-or
          +iented | Scales via distributed master/worker | Smaller reporting ecosystem than k6/JMeter |
      196 +| Gatling | Medium–high — Scala DSL if using core edition | Strong HTTP support; other protocols more
          +limited | Good — Maven/Gradle/sbt plugins | Detailed HTML reports out of the box | High throughput per
          + machine (async engine) | OSS core CLI-only; Enterprise edition paid |
      197 +| Artillery | Low for Node.js-familiar teams | Good HTTP/WebSocket support | Good — npm-based | Basic
          +built-in reporting | Single-machine by default; cloud/serverless execution is a paid add-on | Smaller
          +community/plugin ecosystem than JMeter |
      198 +
      199 +[TODO: licensing/cost column — all five tools have a free, open-source core; paid tiers apply to k6 Cl
          +oud, Gatling Enterprise, and Artillery Cloud for distributed/hosted execution. Confirm current licensi
          +ng terms before citing in the final submission.]
      200 +
      201 +### 7.3 Strengths and Weaknesses
      202 +
      203 +- **k6** — strong scripting ergonomics for JS-familiar teams and strong CI/CD fit; weaker built-in vis
          +ualization without an external dashboard, and no official GUI for less technical users during the in-c
          +lass activity.
      204 +- **Apache JMeter** — very mature and protocol-broad, with a GUI that helps a non-technical audience u
          +nderstand a test plan visually (useful for the in-class activity); GUI-mode execution is explicitly di
          +scouraged for real load generation, and the JVM/thread-per-VU model is heavier on resources than k6's
          +model.
      205 +- **Locust, Gatling, Artillery** — each viable, but less well aligned with either the team's skill set
          + (Locust/Gatling require Python/Scala) or the breadth of protocol/reporting needs (Artillery); conside
          +red as secondary candidates during the survey but not shortlisted (see Section 8).
      206 +
      207  ---

      209  ## 8. Tool Selection
       57 -- Shortlisted tools
       58 -- Final comparison
       59 -- Reasons for selecting Apache JMeter and k6
      210
      211 +### 8.1 Shortlisted Tools
      212 +
      213 +- **Primary:** k6
      214 +- **Secondary:** Apache JMeter
      215 +
      216 +### 8.2 Final Comparison
      217 +
      218 +Both k6 and JMeter satisfy the seminar's mandatory requirement to implement the same workload model in
          + two tools (Section 3.3, item 2). Between the five surveyed candidates (Section 7), k6 and JMeter were
          + selected because they best balance scripting ergonomics, CI-friendliness, and (for JMeter specificall
          +y) GUI-based explainability for a non-technical seminar audience — a factor Locust, Gatling, and Artil
          +lery did not clearly outperform them on for this team's context.
      219 +
      220 +### 8.3 Reasons for Selecting Apache JMeter and k6
      221 +
      222 +- **k6 as primary:** JavaScript scripting matches the team's existing skill set and EShop's own Node.j
          +s stack; k6 is CLI-first, which fits the reproducible, scripted execution needed for the baseline/spik
          +e scenarios (`scripts/run-baseline.sh`, `scripts/run-spike.sh`); k6's built-in `thresholds` API maps d
          +irectly onto the seminar's SLO requirement (p95 < 500 ms, error rate < 1%) without extra tooling.
      223 +- **JMeter as secondary:** its GUI makes the test plan structure (thread groups, samplers, timers, ass
          +ertions) easier to explain visually during the in-class activity and pitch, satisfying the rubric's re
          +quirement to demonstrate a traditional tool feature distinct from the AI-assisted approach; its headle
          +ss CLI mode (`-n -t ... -e -o ...`) still allows a fair, resource-conscious comparison against k6 in S
          +ection 16.
      224 +- **AI direction:** the seminar mandates pairing a traditional tool with an AI-assisted approach. The
          +team uses an LLM to draft a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (`load-tests/data/
          +fake-server.log`, `session.har`), then manually audits the draft against EShop's actual routes and aut
          +hentication requirements (see Section 14).
      225 +

      227
      228  ## 9. Workload Model for EShop
       64 -- User behavior assumptions
       65 -- Workload distribution
       66 -- Baseline scenario
       67 -- Spike scenario
       68 -- Performance criteria (SLO)
      229
      230 +### 9.1 User Behavior Assumptions
      231 +
      232 +The workload model targets four core EShop actions per the seminar brief: Browse/View Product, Search,
          + Add to Cart, and Checkout, following a browse-heavy, checkout-light distribution typical of e-commerc
          +e traffic (most sessions browse or search; a minority proceed to cart and checkout). This distribution
          + is currently an engineering assumption encoded in the shared k6 configuration (`load-tests/k6/lib/con
          +fig.js`); it has **not** yet been cross-checked against the captured HAR/log traffic in `load-tests/da
          +ta/`.
      233 +
      234 +[TODO: validate the assumed mix against `session.har` / `fake-server.log`, or explicitly document it a
          +s a reasoned assumption rather than an observed one, before final submission.]
      235 +
      236 +### 9.2 Workload Distribution
      237 +
      238 +The mix currently implemented in `load-tests/k6/lib/config.js` (`WORKLOAD_MIX`) is:
      239 +
      240 +| Action | Weight | Endpoint |
      241 +|---|---:|---|
      242 +| Browse | 40% | `GET /api/products` |
      243 +| Search | 35% | `GET /api/products?search=<keyword>` |
      244 +| Add to Cart | 20% | `POST /api/cart` (requires auth) |
      245 +| Checkout | 5% | `POST /api/checkout` (requires auth) |
      246 +
      247 +Think time between actions is randomized between 1 and 3 seconds (`randomThinkTimeSeconds()`), consist
          +ent with the sandbox specification (`requirments/sandbox_spec.md`) and intended to approximate realist
          +ic human pacing rather than back-to-back requests.
      248 +
      249 +Search keywords are drawn from the actual seeded product names (`iPhone`, `Samsung`, `MacBook`, `AirPo
          +ds`, `Keychron`) rather than generic English terms, because generic terms return an empty — but still
          +HTTP 200 — result set, which would make the test appear to pass while exercising nothing meaningful.
      250 +
      251 +### 9.3 Baseline Scenario
      252 +
      253 +Defined in `load-tests/k6/baseline.js`: ramp from 0 to 50 virtual users over 1 minute, hold 50 VU for
          +5 minutes, ramp down to 0 over 1 minute (`gracefulRampDown: 30s`). This matches the seminar's baseline
          + requirement (Section 3.3, item 3).
      254 +
      255 +### 9.4 Spike Scenario
      256 +
      257 +Defined in `load-tests/k6/spike.js`: start at 50 VU, ramp to 500 VU over 30 seconds, hold 500 VU for 2
          + minutes, then ramp back down to 50 VU over 1 minute. This matches the seminar's spike requirement (50
          + → 500 VU in 30 s).
      258 +
      259 +### 9.5 Performance Criteria (SLO)
      260 +
      261 +Encoded as k6 thresholds (`SLO_THRESHOLDS` in `lib/config.js`) and mirrored in `load-tests/reports/SLO
          +_Report.md`:
      262 +
      263 +- `http_req_duration`: p95 < 500 ms, p99 < 1000 ms
      264 +- `http_req_failed`: error rate < 1%
      265 +
      266 +Both scenarios require the backend to be started with `LOADTEST=1` to bypass the 200-requests/15-minut
          +es/IP rate limiter (Section 5.2); without this flag, the limiter itself — not application performance
          +— would determine the measured error rate, making the SLO numbers meaningless.
      267 +
      268  ---

      358  ## 13. Implementation using k6
       96 -- Script
       97 -- Configuration
       98 -- Execution
       99 -- Screenshots
      359
      360 +### 13.1 Script
      361 +
      362 +Two scenario scripts exist under `load-tests/k6/`, sharing common configuration from `load-tests/k6/li
          +b/config.js`:
      363 +
      364 +- `baseline.js` — implements the baseline scenario (Section 9.3).
      365 +- `spike.js` — implements the spike scenario (Section 9.4).
      366 +
      367 +Both scripts authenticate once in `setup()` via the shared `login()` helper and pass the resulting tok
          +en to every virtual user, since `/api/cart` and `/api/checkout` require `Authorization: Bearer <token>
          +` (Section 5.3). Each iteration picks an action according to `WORKLOAD_MIX`, sends the corresponding r
          +equest, checks for an HTTP 200 status, and sleeps for a randomized think time.
      368 +
      369 +### 13.2 Configuration
      370 +
      371 +SLO thresholds and the workload mix are centralized in `lib/config.js` (Section 9.2, Section 9.5), avo
          +iding duplication between the baseline and spike scripts. `BASE_URL`, `TEST_EMAIL`, and `TEST_PASSWORD
          +` are overridable via environment variables.
      372 +
      373 +### 13.3 Execution
      374 +
      375 +Per `README.md`, the backend must be started with the rate limiter bypassed:
      376 +
      377 +```bash
      378 +cd eshop/backend && npm install && LOADTEST=1 node server.js
      379 +```
      380 +
      381 +Then, in a separate terminal (k6 must be installed):
      382 +
      383 +```bash
      384 +BASE_URL=http://localhost:3000 ./scripts/run-baseline.sh
      385 +BASE_URL=http://localhost:3000 ./scripts/run-spike.sh
      386 +```

      395
      396  ## 14. AI-Augmented Performance Testing
      104 -- How AI was used
      105 -- Prompts
      106 -- AI-generated artifacts
      107 -- Human review and refinement
      108 -- Evaluation of AI effectiveness
      397
      398 +> Per course policy and `CLAUDE.md`, AI-assisted testing is a **supporting** technique in this seminar
          +, not the main deliverable. The traditional k6/JMeter implementation (Sections 12–13) remains the prim
          +ary technical work; this section documents how an LLM was used to accelerate one specific artifact and
          + how the team verified it.
      399 +
      400 +### 14.1 How AI Was Used
      401 +
      402 +The team's AI direction (per `docs/00_Seminar_Master_Brief.md`, Section 5.2) is to use an LLM to draft
          + a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (`load-tests/data/fake-server.log` and/or `
          +load-tests/data/session.har`), then audit and refine that draft against the real EShop API.
      403 +
      404 +An AI-drafted script already exists at `load-tests/k6/realistic-shopping.ai.js`. It implements a fixed
          + browse → search → add-to-cart → checkout sequence (rather than the weighted random mix used in `basel
          +ine.js`/`spike.js`), ramping from 0 to 20 VU over 1 minute, then to 100 VU over 3 minutes, then down t
          +o 0.
      405 +
      406 +### 14.2 Prompts
      407 +
      408 +[TODO: `ai/prompts/prompts_log.md` is currently empty (template only). The actual prompt text, model u
          +sed, and date must be logged there and summarized here before final submission, per the course's AI di
          +sclosure requirement.]
      409 +
      410 +### 14.3 AI-Generated Artifacts
      411 +
      412 +`realistic-shopping.ai.js` is the primary AI-assisted artifact. It contains an embedded audit checklis
          +t (as a code comment) covering six known categories of AI-scripting mistakes for this SUT:
      413 +
      414 +1. Fabricated endpoints — the seed log referenced `/api/products/search?q=`, which does not exist in E
          +Shop; the real search endpoint is `GET /api/products?search=`. This was corrected in the script.
      415 +2. Missing authentication — AI drafts often omit the login step needed for `/api/cart` and `/api/check
          +out`; a `setup()` login step was added.
      416 +3. Unrealistic action ratios — to be compared against observed log traffic (see the open item in Secti
          +on 9.1).
      417 +4. Missing or incorrect think-time — pacing was set to 1–3 seconds to avoid unrealistic back-to-back r
          +equests.
      418 +5. Hard-coded IDs — flagged as a risk to avoid brittle, non-parameterized product/user/cart IDs.
      419 +6. Weak assertions — the checklist calls out that `status === 200` must be asserted explicitly, since
          +a looser check like `status < 500` would silently accept 401/404/429 responses as "passing."
      420 +
      421 +### 14.4 Human Review and Refinement
      422 +

      495  ## 17. Troubleshooting & Failure Modes
      496
      497  ### Troubleshooting
      142 -- Issues encountered
      143 -- Solutions
      498
      499 +**Issues encountered:** [TODO: log issues actually hit during setup/execution (e.g., rate-limiter 429s
          + if `LOADTEST=1` is forgotten, k6 not installed, JMeter GUI vs. headless discrepancies) with the fix a
          +pplied for each.]
      500 +
      501 +**Solutions:** [TODO — paired with each issue above.]
      502 +
      503  ### Failure Modes
      146 -- Tool limitations
      147 -- AI limitations
      148 -- Common mistakes
      149 -- Recommendations
      504
      505 +The following failure modes are verified directly from EShop's source code (`docs/00_Seminar_Master_Br
          +ief.md`, Section 7) and are safe to cite without running a test, since they describe how the tool/SUT
          +combination can mislead a tester — required content for the User Guide's "Failure Modes" section and d
          +irectly relevant here:
      506 +
      507 +1. **Rate limiter masking real capacity.** EShop applies a 200 requests/15 min/IP limit on all `/api`
          +routes. At 50 or 500 VU, this limiter — not the application's real capacity — would be the first thing
          + hit, producing a wall of HTTP 429 responses and an artificially bad (or misleadingly capped) SLO resu
          +lt. **Detection:** a sudden, sharp rise in 429 responses early in a run. **Mitigation:** start the bac
          +kend with `LOADTEST=1` for every measurement run.
      508 +2. **False-positive "200 OK" on invalid product lookups.** `GET /api/products/:id` for a non-existent
          +product returns HTTP 200 with an empty body (`{}`) instead of 404. A test that asserts only on status
          +code (not body content) will report success while exercising a broken lookup. **Detection:** inspect r
          +esponse bodies, not just status codes, in assertions. **Mitigation:** add body-content checks for prod
          +uct-detail requests.
      509 +3. **Silent search misroute.** `/api/products/search` (without a query string) is not a real search ro
          +ute; it falls into the `:id` route and returns an empty `200` response, while the actual search endpoi
          +nt is `/api/products?search=<keyword>`. A misconfigured script would report 100% success while testing
          + nothing. **Detection:** verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. **Mitigation:** use the
          + correct endpoint and assert on result content, not just status.
      510 +4. **Non-expiring JWTs affecting session assumptions.** `jwt.sign()` is called without `expiresIn`, so
          + tokens never expire. A test script relying on "token expiry mid-test" as a scenario would not observe
          + it; conversely, a long-running test could keep reusing the same token indefinitely, which is unrealis
          +tic for production session behavior. **Detection:** decode a token and check for the absence of an `ex
          +p` claim. **Mitigation:** treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather than an assumption
          + inherited from typical JWT behavior.
      511 +
      512 +[TODO: add AI limitations and common mistakes specific to the AI-generated script, once the human-revi
          +ew evidence in Section 14.4 is complete — e.g., generalize from the fabricated-endpoint and missing-au
          +th corrections already found.]
      513 +
      514 +**Recommendations:** [TODO — to be finalized alongside Section 19.]

      518  ## 18. Research Limitations
      154 -- Environment limitations
      155 -- Dataset limitations
      156 -- Testing scope limitations
      157 -- Threats to validity
      519
      520 +### Environment Limitations
      521 +
      522 +Testing is performed on a local, single-machine setup rather than a representative production deployme
          +nt (no load balancer, no connection pooling, no multi-instance clustering). SQLite's single-writer-con
          +nection model is a known architectural constraint (Section 5.2) that may dominate results in a way tha
          +t would not generalize to a system using a different database.
      523 +
      524 +### Dataset Limitations
      525 +
      526 +The product catalog is small and fixed (5 seeded products), and the captured traffic samples used for
          +AI-assisted script drafting (`session.har`, `fake-server.log`) are limited in size and, in the case of
          + the log file, synthetic rather than fully representative of real user behavior. [TODO: state the actu
          +al size/duration of the HAR capture once reviewed.]
      527 +
      528 +### Testing Scope Limitations
      529 +
      530 +This seminar covers load and spike testing only (Section 4.3); soak/endurance testing, stress-to-failu
          +re testing, and multi-day scalability testing are out of scope due to time constraints. Resource utili
          +zation monitoring may not be captured (Section 15.6), limiting root-cause analysis to latency/error-ra
          +te evidence alone.
      531 +
      532 +### Threats to Validity
      533 +
      534 +- Running the load generator on the same host as the backend under test can inflate measured latency d
          +ue to shared CPU/network resources.
      535 +- The workload mix (Section 9.1) is currently an assumption, not yet validated against the captured HA
          +R/log traffic — a threat to the realism of the "realistic shopping" claim.
      536 +- A single test run per scenario (if that is what occurs) does not account for run-to-run variance; [T
          +ODO: state whether multiple runs were performed and, if not, note this as a limitation.]
      537 +

      185
      186  ### 7.1 Survey of Candidate Tools
      187
      181 -The following general characteristics are drawn from each tool's official documentation and are not sp
          -ecific to EShop; they establish the baseline for the comparison in Section 7.2. [TODO: the team should
          - verify version numbers and add citations to Section 21 (References) before final submission.]
      188 +The following characteristics are drawn from each tool's or product's official documentation and publi
          +c product pages; they are general characteristics, not EShop-specific measurements. [TODO: add officia
          +l references for each tool/product below before final submission — do not cite unverified sources.]
      189
      183 -- **k6** — open-source load testing tool written in Go, with test scripts authored in JavaScript. CLI-
          -first (no built-in GUI), designed for scripted and CI/CD-friendly execution. Provides built-in thresho
          -ld and check APIs for pass/fail SLO assertions, and a text/JSON summary output; richer dashboards are
          -available through the optional k6 Cloud service or third-party exporters (e.g., to Grafana).
      184 -- **Apache JMeter** — mature, Java-based load testing tool (originally released in the late 1990s) wit
          -h both a GUI (for authoring `.jmx` test plans) and a headless/CLI mode (`-n -t ... -l ... -e -o ...`)
          -recommended for actual load generation. Supports a wide range of protocols beyond HTTP (JDBC, JMS, FTP
          -, SOAP) and has a large plugin ecosystem. Generates HTML dashboard reports from CLI runs.
      185 -- **Locust** — Python-based load testing tool where user behavior is defined as Python code rather tha
          -n a DSL or GUI. Supports distributed, master/worker execution to scale beyond a single machine, and pr
          -ovides a web UI for monitoring a running test.
      186 -- **Gatling** — high-performance load testing tool with an async, non-blocking execution engine (Scala
          --based core, with newer Java/JavaScript SDK options). Produces detailed HTML reports and integrates wi
          -th build tools (Maven/Gradle/sbt) for CI. The open-source edition is CLI-only; a paid Enterprise editi
          -on adds a web-based control plane.
      187 -- **Artillery** — Node.js-based load testing tool using YAML or JavaScript test definitions. Lightweig
          -ht and CLI-first, a natural fit for teams already using the Node.js/npm toolchain; a paid cloud add-on
          - offers distributed/serverless load generation.
      190 +**Traditional load-generation tools**
      191
      192 +- **Apache JMeter** — mature, Java-based load testing tool with both a GUI (for authoring `.jmx` test
          +plans) and a headless/CLI mode recommended for actual load generation. Broad protocol support beyond H
          +TTP (JDBC, JMS, FTP, SOAP) and a large plugin ecosystem.
      193 +- **k6** — open-source load testing tool written in Go, scripted in JavaScript. CLI-first, no built-in
          + GUI, designed for scripted and CI/CD-friendly execution. Built-in threshold/check APIs support pass/f
          +ail SLO assertions directly in the script.
      194 +- **Gatling OSS** — high-performance load testing tool with an asynchronous, non-blocking execution en
          +gine. Test scenarios are code-based (Scala DSL in the original core, with newer Java/Kotlin/JavaScript
          + options). The open-source edition is CLI-only and produces detailed HTML reports; distributed executi
          +on and a web-based control plane belong to a separate paid Enterprise edition, outside this seminar's
          +scope.
      195 +
      196 +**AI-assisted directions**
      197 +
      198 +- **ChatGPT/Claude** — general-purpose LLMs used here to draft a k6 script from a captured HTTP log or
          + HAR file. They do not generate load themselves; their output is source code that must be run through
          +k6 like any hand-written script, and reviewed for correctness before use (Section 14).
      199 +- **Grafana k6 AI** — an AI-assisted feature within the Grafana/k6 ecosystem intended to suggest reque
          +st correlation and threshold values from a recorded session. It operates on top of k6's own execution
          +engine rather than as a standalone tool.
      200 +- **Akamas** — a commercial, ML-based optimization platform that tunes system/application parameters (
          +e.g., JVM, database, infrastructure settings) against observed performance data. Per the seminar brief
          +, Akamas is included for discussion only in this seminar and is not executed against EShop, since it o
          +ptimizes an already-running system rather than generating load.

          - dashboard report (`-e -o`) | Higher per-VU memory/CPU overhead due to JVM + thread-per-VU model | Mat

          -community/plugin ecosystem than JMeter |
      206 +| Apache JMeter | Traditional | Medium — GUI eases entry, `.jmx` XML is verbose | Very broad (HTTP, JD
          +BC, JMS, FTP, SOAP, etc.) | Good in headless (`-n`) mode; GUI mode is **not** suitable for real load |
          + Built-in HTML dashboard report (`-e -o`) | Higher per-VU resource overhead (JVM, thread-per-VU) |
      207 +| k6 | Traditional | Low–medium for JS-familiar teams | Strong HTTP/1.1 & HTTP/2; WebSocket, gRPC supp
          +ort | Strong — CLI-first, Docker-friendly | Built-in summary + thresholds; richer dashboards via Grafa
          +na/k6 Cloud | Good on a single machine; distributed/cloud execution needs an external service |
      208 +| Gatling OSS | Traditional | Medium–high — code-based DSL | Strong HTTP support; other protocols more

          +throughput per machine (async engine); OSS edition is single-machine |
      209 +| ChatGPT/Claude | AI-assisted | Low to start, but output requires review | N/A — produces script code
          + that targets another tool's protocol support | N/A — used offline to draft scripts, not itself part o
          +f a pipeline | N/A — no test reporting; produces code artifacts only | N/A — not a load generator |

          +pport | Inherits k6's CI/CD fit | Assists with correlation/threshold suggestions, not full reporting |

          +d generator; discussion only (Section 7.1) |

      215  ### 7.3 Strengths and Weaknesses
      216
      203 -- **k6** — strong scripting ergonomics for JS-familiar teams and strong CI/CD fit; weaker built-in vis
          -ualization without an external dashboard, and no official GUI for less technical users during the in-c
          -lass activity.
      204 -- **Apache JMeter** — very mature and protocol-broad, with a GUI that helps a non-technical audience u
          -nderstand a test plan visually (useful for the in-class activity); GUI-mode execution is explicitly di
          -scouraged for real load generation, and the JVM/thread-per-VU model is heavier on resources than k6's
          -model.
      205 -- **Locust, Gatling, Artillery** — each viable, but less well aligned with either the team's skill set
          - (Locust/Gatling require Python/Scala) or the breadth of protocol/reporting needs (Artillery); conside
          -red as secondary candidates during the survey but not shortlisted (see Section 8).
      217 +- **Apache JMeter** — mature, protocol-broad, and its GUI helps explain a test plan visually to a non-
          +technical audience; GUI-mode execution is not suitable for real load, and the JVM/thread-per-VU model
          +is heavier on resources than k6's.
      218 +- **k6** — strong scripting ergonomics for a JS-familiar team and strong CI/CD fit; no official GUI, s
          +o a purely CLI-based demo may be less approachable for a non-technical audience without additional exp
          +lanation.
      219 +- **Gatling OSS** — high throughput per machine and strong built-in reporting, but its code-based, Sca
          +la-first DSL is a steeper learning curve for this team than k6's JavaScript.
      220 +- **ChatGPT/Claude** — fast at producing a first-draft script, but prone to concrete, verifiable mista
          +kes (Section 14.3: fabricated endpoints, missing authentication) and requires mandatory human audit be
          +fore use.
      221 +- **Grafana k6 AI** — convenient if already committed to the k6/Grafana ecosystem, but is an assistive
          + feature rather than an independent capability, and its suggestions still require verification like an
          +y AI output.
      222 +- **Akamas** — addresses a different problem (tuning an already-running system's parameters) than this
          + seminar's workload-generation focus; useful to discuss as a complementary AI direction, but not execu
          +ted against EShop in this seminar.
      223
      224 +No single candidate is treated as universally better than the others; each fits a different role in th
          +is seminar (Section 8), and the comparison above reflects general, documentation-based characteristics
          + rather than EShop-specific measurements.

      237  ### 8.2 Final Comparison
      238
      218 -Both k6 and JMeter satisfy the seminar's mandatory requirement to implement the same workload model in
          - two tools (Section 3.3, item 2). Between the five surveyed candidates (Section 7), k6 and JMeter were
          - selected because they best balance scripting ergonomics, CI-friendliness, and (for JMeter specificall
          -y) GUI-based explainability for a non-technical seminar audience — a factor Locust, Gatling, and Artil
          -lery did not clearly outperform them on for this team's context.
      239 +Both k6 and JMeter satisfy the seminar's mandatory requirement to implement the same workload model in
          + two tools (Section 3.3, item 2). Among the traditional candidates (Section 7), Gatling OSS was not sh
          +ortlisted primarily because of its steeper, code-based Scala-first learning curve relative to the team
          +'s existing JavaScript skill set — not because it is a weaker tool in general. Among the AI-assisted c
          +andidates, ChatGPT/Claude was selected as the seminar's AI direction because it pairs directly with th
          +e code-based scripting workflow already chosen for k6, while Grafana k6 AI and Akamas were kept as dis
          +cussion points rather than executed candidates (Section 7.1, Section 7.3).
      240
      241  ### 8.3 Reasons for Selecting Apache JMeter and k6
      242
      222 -- **k6 as primary:** JavaScript scripting matches the team's existing skill set and EShop's own Node.j
          -s stack; k6 is CLI-first, which fits the reproducible, scripted execution needed for the baseline/spik
          -e scenarios (`scripts/run-baseline.sh`, `scripts/run-spike.sh`); k6's built-in `thresholds` API maps d
          -irectly onto the seminar's SLO requirement (p95 < 500 ms, error rate < 1%) without extra tooling.
      223 -- **JMeter as secondary:** its GUI makes the test plan structure (thread groups, samplers, timers, ass
          -ertions) easier to explain visually during the in-class activity and pitch, satisfying the rubric's re
          -quirement to demonstrate a traditional tool feature distinct from the AI-assisted approach; its headle
          -ss CLI mode (`-n -t ... -e -o ...`) still allows a fair, resource-conscious comparison against k6 in S
          -ection 16.
      224 -- **AI direction:** the seminar mandates pairing a traditional tool with an AI-assisted approach. The
          -team uses an LLM to draft a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (`load-tests/data/
          -fake-server.log`, `session.har`), then manually audits the draft against EShop's actual routes and aut
          -hentication requirements (see Section 14).
      243 +- **k6 as primary — suited to code-based scripting and AI assistance.** k6 scripts are plain JavaScrip
          +t files, which an LLM can draft and a human can review using the same language and mental model, with
          +no translation step between what the AI produces and what the tool executes. This directly supports th
          +e seminar's required AI-assisted direction (Section 3.3, item 5; Section 14). k6 is also CLI-first, fi
          +tting the reproducible, scripted execution needed for the baseline/spike scenarios (`scripts/run-basel
          +ine.sh`, `scripts/run-spike.sh`), and its built-in `thresholds` API maps directly onto the seminar's S
          +LO requirement (p95 < 500 ms, error rate < 1%) without extra tooling.
      244 +- **JMeter as secondary — a standard, mature, GUI-supported tool.** JMeter is included because it is a
          + long-established, widely taught performance testing tool whose GUI makes the test plan structure (thr
          +ead groups, samplers, timers, assertions) easier to explain visually during the in-class activity and
          +pitch — a role a purely code-based tool does not fill as directly. This satisfies the rubric's require
          +ment to demonstrate a traditional tool feature distinct from the AI-assisted approach. Its headless CL
          +I mode (`-n -t ... -e -o ...`) still allows a fair, resource-conscious comparison against k6 in Sectio
          +n 16.
      245 +- **Why not treat one as strictly better:** k6 and JMeter serve different needs in this seminar — k6 f
          +or scripted, AI-paired, CI-style execution, and JMeter for GUI-based explainability and protocol bread
          +th. Section 16 evaluates both empirically once test data is available, rather than assuming either is
          +superior in general.
      246 +- **AI direction:** the seminar mandates pairing a traditional tool with an AI-assisted approach. The
          +team uses an LLM (ChatGPT/Claude) to draft a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (
          +`load-tests/data/fake-server.log`, `session.har`), then manually audits the draft against EShop's actu
          +al routes and authentication requirements (Section 14). Grafana k6 AI and Akamas remain documented as
          +considered alternatives (Section 7.1) but are not executed in this seminar's scope.

❯ We have clarified the intended workflow of this seminar. Please refactor `@report/report.md` accordingly.

  First, understand these principles before making any changes.

  # 1. Final Report must be a standalone document

  The report will be read independently.

  Remove all references that depend on our project repository structure, for example:

  - docs/...
  - load-tests/...
  - scripts/...
  - ai/...
  - README.md
  - CLAUDE.md
  - baseline.js
  - spike.js
  - lib/config.js
  - *.md
  - *.jmx
  - *.js
  - *.sh

  Instead:

  - describe the implementation naturally,
  - reference only report sections, figures, tables, appendices and academic references.

  The reader should never need to know our folder structure.

  ---

  # 2. Tool Survey Proposal is already a separate approved deliverable

  `S1_Tool_Survey_Proposal.md` has already been submitted and approved.

  The Final Report is NOT a duplicate of the proposal.

  Therefore:

  REMOVE:

  - Tool Survey Methodology
  - Performance Testing Tool Survey

  Reduce Tool Selection into a very short summary explaining only why Apache JMeter and k6 were selected for this
  seminar.

  Do NOT repeat the comparison matrix.

  Do NOT repeat candidate tool analysis.

  Do NOT repeat licensing/community comparisons.

  ---

  # 3. User Guide is a separate deliverable

  `S4_User_Guide.md` is another required artifact.

  Therefore:

  Implementation sections inside the report are NOT user manuals.

  Remove any content that belongs to a User Guide, including:

  - installation steps
  - exact shell commands
  - screenshots placeholders
  - troubleshooting procedures
  - first test walkthrough
  - advanced usage
  - detailed configuration instructions

  Implementation sections should only explain:

  - how the tool was applied in this seminar
  - how the workload model was implemented
  - important implementation decisions

  They should read like an implementation summary, not a tutorial.

  ---

  # 4. Remove development-only wording

  Delete all wording intended only for report drafting, including:

  - [TODO: ...]
  - "this draft"
  - "pending"
  - "to be filled later"
  - references to missing artifacts
  - explanations that something has not yet been implemented

  If some information is unavailable, leave a concise placeholder instead of discussing the writing process.

  ---

  # 5. Simplify the report

  The report should focus on the seminar itself.

  Avoid repeating information already covered by:

  - Tool Survey Proposal
  - User Guide

  Keep only information necessary to understand:

  - Performance Testing concepts
  - EShop
  - Workload Model
  - Experimental Methodology
  - AI-assisted workflow
  - Experimental Results
  - Tool Evaluation
  - Discussion
  - Conclusion

  ---

  # 6. Preserve

  Do NOT rewrite everything.

  Keep sections that are already good, including:

  - Performance Testing Fundamentals
  - EShop System
  - Workload Model
  - Experimental Methodology
  - AI-assisted Performance Testing

  Only remove duplicated or misplaced content.

  The goal is to transform it into a clean, standalone academic report that complements the Proposal and User Guide
  instead of duplicating them.

      101  ## 5. EShop System & Testing Scope
      102
      103 -> This section reflects source-code-level facts about EShop that the team has verified by reading `esh
          -op/backend/server.js` and `database.js` (see `docs/00_Seminar_Master_Brief.md`, Section 7). It does no
          -t yet include measured runtime behavior — that belongs to Section 15.
      104 -
      103  ### 5.1 EShop Overview
      104
      107 -EShop is a small e-commerce web application built with Express (Node.js) and SQLite (`database.sqlite`
          -), listening on port 3000. It exposes a REST API for authentication, product browsing/search, cart man
          -agement, and checkout, backed by a seeded catalog of five products (IDs 1–5) and two seeded accounts (
          -a regular user and an admin).
      105 +EShop is a small e-commerce web application built on a Node.js/Express backend with a SQLite database.
          + It exposes a REST API for authentication, product browsing and search, cart management, and checkout,
          + backed by a small seeded product catalog and two seeded user accounts (a regular user and an administ
          +rator).
      106
      107  ### 5.2 System Architecture
      108
      111 -- **Backend:** Express.js application, single Node.js process.
      112 -- **Database:** SQLite, a single-file, single-writer-connection database. `initDatabase()` runs uncond
          -itionally on every server start, dropping and re-seeding the schema — this gives the team a "clean bas
          -eline" for free (restart the backend before each measurement round) but also means the database is not
          - durable across restarts.
      113 -- **Authentication:** JWT-based login. Note: `jwt.sign()` is called without an `expiresIn` option, so
          -tokens do not expire — relevant to how long a token can be reused across a load test session.
      114 -- **State:** the shopping cart (`userCarts`) is held in server process memory, not persisted to SQLite
          -; it resets on restart and can grow unbounded across repeated test runs.
      115 -- **Rate limiting:** a global limiter of 200 requests / 15 minutes / IP applies to all `/api` routes.
          -This is the single most important constraint for load testing EShop — at 50 or 500 virtual users, the
          -limiter would return HTTP 429 after roughly 200 requests, invalidating any SLO measurement, unless byp
          -assed. The backend supports a `LOADTEST=1` startup flag specifically to disable this limiter for measu
          -rement runs.
      109 +- **Backend:** a single-process Express.js application.
      110 +- **Database:** SQLite, which serializes writes through a single connection. The schema is reset and r
          +e-seeded on every server restart, which provides a consistent starting state for each measurement roun
          +d but means the database is not durable across restarts.
      111 +- **Authentication:** JWT-based login. Issued tokens do not expire, which affects how long a session t
          +oken can remain valid and reusable across a load test.
      112 +- **State:** the shopping cart is held in server memory rather than the database; it resets on restart
          + and can grow unbounded across repeated test runs.
      113 +- **Rate limiting:** a global limiter caps each client IP address to 200 requests within a 15-minute w
          +indow, applied across all API routes. This is the single most important constraint for load testing ES
          +hop: at 50 or 500 virtual users, the limiter would be reached long before the system's real capacity,
          +invalidating any measurement unless it is disabled for the duration of the test. The backend provides
          +a dedicated testing configuration to disable the limiter during measurement runs.
      114
      115  ### 5.3 Testing Scope
      116
      119 -The performance test targets the following EShop flows, matching the workload model required by the se
          -minar brief:
      117 +The performance test targets four core flows, matching the workload model in Section 7:
      118
      121 -- **Browse** — `GET /api/products` (product listing).
      122 -- **Search** — `GET /api/products?search=<keyword>` (parameterized `LIKE` query; note the real search
          -endpoint is `/api/products?search=`, not `/api/products/search`, which instead falls through to the `:
          -id` route and returns an empty `200` response).
      123 -- **Add to Cart** — `POST /api/cart`, requires `Authorization: Bearer <token>`.
      124 -- **Checkout** — `POST /api/checkout`, requires `Authorization: Bearer <token>`; the endpoint trusts t
          -he client-submitted `total_amount` without server-side recalculation.
      119 +- **Browse** — retrieving the product listing.
      120 +- **Search** — a parameterized, keyword-based product search.
      121 +- **Add to Cart** — adding a product to an authenticated user's cart.
      122 +- **Checkout** — submitting an authenticated order.
      123
      126 -Out of scope: admin-only endpoints, non-HTTP protocols, and any UI-level (browser) testing — this is a
          -n API-level performance test.
      124 +Two behavioral details are relevant to test design: a request for a non-existent product returns a suc
          +cessful (HTTP 200) response with an empty body rather than an error status, so assertions must check r
          +esponse content rather than status code alone; and the checkout flow accepts the order total supplied
          +by the client without independently recalculating it.
      125
      126 +Out of scope: administrative endpoints, non-HTTP protocols, and browser/UI-level testing — this is an
          +API-level performance test.

      132 -### 5.5 Limitations of the Environment (Node.js + SQLite + Local)
      132 +### 5.5 Limitations of the Environment
      133
      134 -- SQLite serializes writes through a single connection; under concurrent checkout/cart writes, content
          -ion at the database layer is expected to be the primary bottleneck rather than the HTTP layer — this i
          -s a hypothesis to be confirmed in Section 15, not yet a measured result.
      135 -- Running the load generator (k6/JMeter) on the same machine as the backend under test can introduce r
          -esource contention that skews latency measurements upward; this is a known threat to validity, tracked
          - in Section 18.
      136 -- The environment is local and single-instance; it does not represent a production deployment with loa
          -d balancing, connection pooling, or a multi-core/clustered Node.js process.
      134 +- SQLite's single-writer-connection model means database-layer contention, rather than the HTTP layer,
          + is the expected bottleneck under concurrent cart/checkout writes — a hypothesis evaluated in Section

      177 -- **Apache JMeter** — mature, Java-based load testing tool with both a GUI (for authoring `.jmx` test
          -plans) and a headless/CLI mode recommended for actual load generation. Broad protocol support beyond H
          -TTP (JDBC, JMS, FTP, SOAP) and a large plugin ecosystem.
      178 -- **k6** — open-source load testing tool written in Go, scripted in JavaScript. CLI-first, no built-in
          - GUI, designed for scripted and CI/CD-friendly execution. Built-in threshold/check APIs support pass/f
          -ail SLO assertions directly in the script.
      179 -- **Gatling OSS** — high-performance load testing tool with an asynchronous, non-blocking execution en
          -gine. Test scenarios are code-based (Scala DSL in the original core, with newer Java/Kotlin/JavaScript
          - options). The open-source edition is CLI-only and produces detailed HTML reports; distributed executi
          -on and a web-based control plane belong to a separate paid Enterprise edition, outside this seminar's
          -scope.

          - HAR file. They do not generate load themselves; their output is source code that must be run through
          -k6 like any hand-written script, and reviewed for correctness before use (Section 14).
      184 -- **Grafana k6 AI** — an AI-assisted feature within the Grafana/k6 ecosystem intended to suggest reque
          -st correlation and threshold values from a recorded session. It operates on top of k6's own execution
          -engine rather than as a standalone tool.

          -e.g., JVM, database, infrastructure settings) against observed performance data. Per the seminar brief
          -, Akamas is included for discussion only in this seminar and is not executed against EShop, since it o
          -ptimizes an already-running system rather than generating load.

      200 -### 7.3 Strengths and Weaknesses
      201 -
      202 -- **Apache JMeter** — mature, protocol-broad, and its GUI helps explain a test plan visually to a non-
          -technical audience; GUI-mode execution is not suitable for real load, and the JVM/thread-per-VU model
          -is heavier on resources than k6's.
      203 -- **k6** — strong scripting ergonomics for a JS-familiar team and strong CI/CD fit; no official GUI, s
          -o a purely CLI-based demo may be less approachable for a non-technical audience without additional exp
          -lanation.
      204 -- **Gatling OSS** — high throughput per machine and strong built-in reporting, but its code-based, Sca
          -la-first DSL is a steeper learning curve for this team than k6's JavaScript.
      205 -- **ChatGPT/Claude** — fast at producing a first-draft script, but prone to concrete, verifiable mista
          -kes (Section 14.3: fabricated endpoints, missing authentication) and requires mandatory human audit be
          -fore use.
      206 -- **Grafana k6 AI** — convenient if already committed to the k6/Grafana ecosystem, but is an assistive
          - feature rather than an independent capability, and its suggestions still require verification like an
          -y AI output.
      207 -- **Akamas** — addresses a different problem (tuning an already-running system's parameters) than this
          - seminar's workload-generation focus; useful to discuss as a complementary AI direction, but not execu
          -ted against EShop in this seminar.

          -is seminar (Section 8), and the comparison above reflects general, documentation-based characteristics
          - rather than EShop-specific measurements.

      213 -## 8. Tool Selection
      214 -
      215 -### 8.1 Shortlisted Tools
      216 -
      217 -- **Primary traditional tool:** k6
      218 -- **Secondary traditional tool:** Apache JMeter
      219 -- **AI-assisted direction:** ChatGPT/Claude-based script generation, audited by the team (Section 14)
      220 -- **Discussed but not executed:** Grafana k6 AI, Akamas (Section 7.1)
      221 -
      222 -### 8.2 Final Comparison
      223 -
      224 -Both k6 and JMeter satisfy the seminar's mandatory requirement to implement the same workload model in
          - two tools (Section 3.3, item 2). Among the traditional candidates (Section 7), Gatling OSS was not sh
          -ortlisted primarily because of its steeper, code-based Scala-first learning curve relative to the team
          -'s existing JavaScript skill set — not because it is a weaker tool in general. Among the AI-assisted c
          -andidates, ChatGPT/Claude was selected as the seminar's AI direction because it pairs directly with th
          -e code-based scripting workflow already chosen for k6, while Grafana k6 AI and Akamas were kept as dis
          -cussion points rather than executed candidates (Section 7.1, Section 7.3).
      225 -
      226 -### 8.3 Reasons for Selecting Apache JMeter and k6
      227 -
      228 -- **k6 as primary — suited to code-based scripting and AI assistance.** k6 scripts are plain JavaScrip
          -t files, which an LLM can draft and a human can review using the same language and mental model, with
          -no translation step between what the AI produces and what the tool executes. This directly supports th
          -e seminar's required AI-assisted direction (Section 3.3, item 5; Section 14). k6 is also CLI-first, fi
          -tting the reproducible, scripted execution needed for the baseline/spike scenarios (`scripts/run-basel
          -ine.sh`, `scripts/run-spike.sh`), and its built-in `thresholds` API maps directly onto the seminar's S
          -LO requirement (p95 < 500 ms, error rate < 1%) without extra tooling.
      229 -- **JMeter as secondary — a standard, mature, GUI-supported tool.** JMeter is included because it is a
          - long-established, widely taught performance testing tool whose GUI makes the test plan structure (thr
          -ead groups, samplers, timers, assertions) easier to explain visually during the in-class activity and
          -pitch — a role a purely code-based tool does not fill as directly. This satisfies the rubric's require
          -ment to demonstrate a traditional tool feature distinct from the AI-assisted approach. Its headless CL
          -I mode (`-n -t ... -e -o ...`) still allows a fair, resource-conscious comparison against k6 in Sectio
          -n 16.
      230 -- **Why not treat one as strictly better:** k6 and JMeter serve different needs in this seminar — k6 f
          -or scripted, AI-paired, CI-style execution, and JMeter for GUI-based explainability and protocol bread
          -th. Section 16 evaluates both empirically once test data is available, rather than assuming either is
          -superior in general.
      231 -- **AI direction:** the seminar mandates pairing a traditional tool with an AI-assisted approach. The
          -team uses an LLM (ChatGPT/Claude) to draft a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (
          -`load-tests/data/fake-server.log`, `session.har`), then manually audits the draft against EShop's actu
          -al routes and authentication requirements (Section 14). Grafana k6 AI and Akamas remain documented as
          -considered alternatives (Section 7.1) but are not executed in this seminar's scope.

      391 -### 12.1 Test Plan
      238 +### 10.1 Implementation Summary
      239
      393 -A test plan skeleton exists at `load-tests/jmeter/eshop-perf.jmx`, guided by `load-tests/jmeter/README
          -.md`. It is intended to implement the **same workload model** as the k6 scripts (Section 9, Section 10
          -.1) — not a separate or looser approximation of it. The planned structure is:
      240 +The JMeter test plan reproduces the workload model in Section 7 as a set of thread groups — one for th
          +e baseline scenario and one for the spike scenario — each generating the same action mix (browse, sear
          +ch, add-to-cart, checkout) against the same endpoints as the k6 implementation, with equivalent timers
          + to reproduce the randomized think time and equivalent ramp/hold/ramp-down timing. Authentication is p
          +erformed once per session and reused across requests, since cart and checkout actions require an authe
          +nticated session. A non-GUI execution mode is used for all measured runs, consistent with general prac
          +tice that GUI-mode execution is not representative of real load-generation performance.
      241
      395 -1. Thread Group(s) for the baseline and spike scenarios (mirroring the VU/ramp parameters in Section 1
          -0.6).
      396 -2. HTTP Request Defaults, pointing at the `BASE_URL` host/port.
      397 -3. CSV Data Set Config (optional), for parameterized search keywords or product IDs, mirroring the k6
          -`SharedArray` lists.
      398 -4. HTTP Samplers for the four workload actions (browse, search, cart, checkout), including the `Author
          -ization: Bearer` header for cart/checkout requests.
      399 -5. Timers to reproduce the 1–3 second randomized think time.
      400 -6. Assertions on response status and, where relevant, response body (to catch the "200 with empty body
          -" traps described in Section 5.3).
      401 -7. Summary Report / Aggregate Report listeners for the headless run.
      242 +### 10.2 Key Implementation Decisions
      243
      403 -### 12.2 Configuration

          +s a successful but empty response (Section 5.3).
      245 +- Search keywords are parameterized from the same seeded product names used in the k6 implementation,
          +so both tools exercise equivalent, non-trivial search results.
      246 +- Results are collected through built-in aggregate reporting rather than a custom listener, keeping th
          +e two tools' reporting comparable in Section 14.

      427 -### 13.1 Script
      254 +### 11.1 Implementation Summary
      255
      429 -Two scenario scripts exist under `load-tests/k6/`, sharing common configuration from `load-tests/k6/li
          -b/config.js`:
      256 +The k6 implementation consists of two scripts sharing a common configuration: one for the baseline sce
          +nario and one for the spike scenario (Section 7.3–7.4). Both authenticate once at the start of the tes
          +t and share the resulting session token across all virtual users, since cart and checkout actions requ
          +ire authentication. Each simulated user iteration selects an action according to the workload distribu
          +tion in Section 7.2, sends the corresponding request, checks for a successful response, and pauses for
          + a randomized think time before the next iteration.
      257
      431 -- `baseline.js` — implements the baseline scenario (Section 9.3, Section 10.6).
      432 -- `spike.js` — implements the spike scenario (Section 9.4, Section 10.6).
      258 +### 11.2 Key Implementation Decisions
      259
      434 -Both scripts implement the **same workload model** that the JMeter test plan is intended to mirror (Se
          -ction 10.1, Section 12.1). Both authenticate once in `setup()` via the shared `login()` helper and pas
          -s the resulting token to every virtual user, since `/api/cart` and `/api/checkout` require `Authorizat
          -ion: Bearer <token>` (Section 5.3). Each iteration picks an action according to `WORKLOAD_MIX`, sends
          -the corresponding request, checks for an HTTP 200 status, and sleeps for a randomized think time.
      260 +- Thresholds for p95/p99 latency and error rate (Section 7.5) are declared directly in the script and
          +evaluated automatically at the end of each run, rather than computed manually afterward.
      261 +- Configuration (target host, credentials, thresholds, workload mix) is centralized in a shared module
          + to avoid duplicating logic between the baseline and spike scripts.
      262 +- Search keywords and product identifiers are drawn from the seeded catalog rather than hard-coded arb
          +itrary values, avoiding a false-positive "empty but successful" result (Section 5.3).
      263

          -` are overridable via environment variables. [TODO: record the k6 version used (Section 10.4).]

      271
      469 -### 14.1 How AI Was Used
      272 +### 12.1 How AI Was Used
      273
      471 -The team's AI direction (per `docs/00_Seminar_Master_Brief.md`, Section 5.2) is to use an LLM to draft
          - a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log (`load-tests/data/fake-server.log` and/or `
          -load-tests/data/session.har`), then audit and refine that draft against the real EShop API.
      274 +An LLM was used to draft a "realistic shopping" k6 script from a captured HTTP log, representing a sin
          +gle shopping session (browse → search → add-to-cart → checkout), which the team then audited and corre
          +cted against the real system before treating it as usable.
      275
      473 -An AI-drafted script already exists at `load-tests/k6/realistic-shopping.ai.js`. It implements a fixed
          - browse → search → add-to-cart → checkout sequence (rather than the weighted random mix used in `basel
          -ine.js`/`spike.js`), ramping from 0 to 20 VU over 1 minute, then to 100 VU over 3 minutes, then down t

      276 +### 12.2 Prompts

      475 -### 14.2 Prompts

      279
      477 -[TODO: `ai/prompts/prompts_log.md` is currently empty (template only). The actual prompt text, model u
          -sed, and date must be logged there and summarized here before final submission, per the course's AI di
          -sclosure requirement.]
      280 +### 12.3 Audit Findings
      281
      479 -### 14.3 AI-Generated Artifacts
      282 +Reviewing the AI-drafted script against the real system surfaced a recurring set of mistake categories
          +, consistent with known risks of LLM-generated test scripts:
      283
      481 -`realistic-shopping.ai.js` is the primary AI-assisted artifact. It contains an embedded audit checklis
          -t (as a code comment) covering six known categories of AI-scripting mistakes for this SUT:
      284 +1. **Fabricated endpoint.** The draft referenced a search endpoint that does not exist in the system;
          +the correct endpoint uses a different path pattern. Corrected after comparison with the verified API.
      285 +2. **Missing authentication.** The draft omitted the login step required before cart and checkout requ
          +ests; a setup step was added to obtain and reuse a session token.
      286 +3. **Unrealistic action ratios.** The draft's fixed sequential flow does not reflect the browse-heavy,
          + checkout-light distribution used elsewhere in this study (Section 7.2); reconciling the two is an ope
          +n item.
      287 +4. **Think-time and pacing.** The draft required an explicit pause between actions to avoid back-to-ba
          +ck requests; pacing was set to 1–3 seconds.
      288 +5. **Hard-coded identifiers.** The draft referenced fixed product/user identifiers rather than paramet
          +erized values, a brittleness risk flagged during review.
      289 +6. **Weak assertions.** The draft's success checks needed tightening to assert an exact successful sta
          +tus rather than a looser range that would silently accept authentication or rate-limiting failures as
          +passing.
      290
      483 -1. Fabricated endpoints — the seed log referenced `/api/products/search?q=`, which does not exist in E
          -Shop; the real search endpoint is `GET /api/products?search=`. This was corrected in the script.
      484 -2. Missing authentication — AI drafts often omit the login step needed for `/api/cart` and `/api/check
          -out`; a `setup()` login step was added.
      485 -3. Unrealistic action ratios — to be compared against observed log traffic (see the open item in Secti
          -on 9.1).
      486 -4. Missing or incorrect think-time — pacing was set to 1–3 seconds to avoid unrealistic back-to-back r
          -equests.
      487 -5. Hard-coded IDs — flagged as a risk to avoid brittle, non-parameterized product/user/cart IDs.
      488 -6. Weak assertions — the checklist calls out that `status === 200` must be asserted explicitly, since
          -a looser check like `status < 500` would silently accept 401/404/429 responses as "passing."
      291 +### 12.4 Human Review and Refinement
      292
      490 -### 14.4 Human Review and Refinement

          +able fixes applied directly to the script. [Document the remaining audit items with before/after evide
          +nce.]

      592 -**Issues encountered:** [TODO: log issues actually hit during setup/execution (e.g., rate-limiter 429s

      383 +The following failure modes describe ways the tool/system combination can mislead a tester, verified d
          +irectly against the system's behavior rather than assumed:
      384
      594 -**Solutions:** [TODO — paired with each issue above.]
      385 +1. **Rate limiter masking real capacity.** The application-level rate limiter, if left enabled, is rea
          +ched long before the system's real capacity at 50 or 500 virtual users, producing a wall of rejected r
          +equests and an artificially capped result. *Detection:* a sharp, early rise in rejected requests. *Mit
          +igation:* disable the limiter for the duration of measurement (Section 5.2).
      386 +2. **False-positive success on invalid lookups.** A request for a non-existent product returns a succe
          +ssful response with an empty body rather than an error status. *Detection:* assertions must inspect re
          +sponse content, not status code alone. *Mitigation:* add content-based checks for product-detail reque
          +sts.
      387 +3. **Silent search misroute.** An incorrectly formed search request can silently fall through to an un
          +related route and return an empty, successful response, so a misconfigured script can report full succ
          +ess while testing nothing. *Detection:* verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. *Mitigat
          +ion:* validate the endpoint pattern and assert on result content.
      388 +4. **Non-expiring session tokens.** Authentication tokens do not expire once issued, so a script relyi
          +ng on mid-test token expiry will not observe it, and a long-running test may reuse the same token inde
          +finitely — unrealistic compared to typical session behavior. *Detection:* inspect issued tokens for th
          +e absence of an expiry claim. *Mitigation:* treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather
          +than an inherited assumption.

      392 +### 15.2 Environment and Dataset Limitations
      393
      600 -1. **Rate limiter masking real capacity.** EShop applies a 200 requests/15 min/IP limit on all `/api`
          -routes. At 50 or 500 VU, this limiter — not the application's real capacity — would be the first thing
          - hit, producing a wall of HTTP 429 responses and an artificially bad (or misleadingly capped) SLO resu
          -lt. **Detection:** a sudden, sharp rise in 429 responses early in a run. **Mitigation:** start the bac
          -kend with `LOADTEST=1` for every measurement run.
      601 -2. **False-positive "200 OK" on invalid product lookups.** `GET /api/products/:id` for a non-existent
          -product returns HTTP 200 with an empty body (`{}`) instead of 404. A test that asserts only on status
          -code (not body content) will report success while exercising a broken lookup. **Detection:** inspect r
          -esponse bodies, not just status codes, in assertions. **Mitigation:** add body-content checks for prod
          -uct-detail requests.
      602 -3. **Silent search misroute.** `/api/products/search` (without a query string) is not a real search ro
          -ute; it falls into the `:id` route and returns an empty `200` response, while the actual search endpoi
          -nt is `/api/products?search=<keyword>`. A misconfigured script would report 100% success while testing
          - nothing. **Detection:** verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. **Mitigation:** use the
          - correct endpoint and assert on result content, not just status.
      603 -4. **Non-expiring JWTs affecting session assumptions.** `jwt.sign()` is called without `expiresIn`, so
          - tokens never expire. A test script relying on "token expiry mid-test" as a scenario would not observe
          - it; conversely, a long-running test could keep reusing the same token indefinitely, which is unrealis
          -tic for production session behavior. **Detection:** decode a token and check for the absence of an `ex
          -p` claim. **Mitigation:** treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather than an assumption
          - inherited from typical JWT behavior.

          +nt (no load balancing, connection pooling, or multi-instance clustering). The database's single-writer
          +-connection model is a known architectural constraint (Section 5.2) that may dominate results in a way
          + that would not generalize to a system built on a different database. The product catalog is small and
          + fixed, and the traffic sample used for the AI-assisted scripting workflow (Section 12) is limited in
          +size and partly synthetic rather than fully representative of real user behavior.

  licensing/community talk.

  implementation sections — those are now short "Implementation Summary" + "Key Implementation Decisions" write-ups, not
  tutorials.
  - The "Troubleshooting" (issues/solutions) subsection — that's User Guide territory.
  - Every repo-structure reference: no more docs/..., load-tests/..., scripts/..., ai/..., README.md, CLAUDE.md,
  .js/.sh/.jmx/.md filenames, or code identifiers like jwt.sign(), WORKLOAD_MIX, LOADTEST=1. Behavior is now described
  in plain English (e.g., "a global limiter caps each client IP to 200 requests within a 15-minute window" instead of
  citing the env flag).

  installed") — replaced with short imperative placeholders like [Add ...], [Confirm ...], [State ...], or — in tables.

❯ Update `@report/report.md`.

  The report is becoming too detailed in several places. Remember that this is a **Software Testing seminar report**,
  not an academic research paper.

  Refactor Sections **7 (Workload Model for EShop)** and **8 (Experimental Methodology)** using the following
  principles.

  ---

  # 1. Workload Model

  Keep only the information necessary to explain the workload design.

  Keep:

  - rationale of the workload model (very concise)
  - workload distribution table
  - think time
  - baseline workload
  - spike workload
  - performance metrics / SLO

  Remove unnecessary implementation details, for example:

  - search keywords
  - implementation-specific explanations
  - endpoint-level discussions
  - assumptions that are not important to understanding the workload
  - long paragraphs describing why certain keywords were chosen

  The reader only needs to understand:

  - what the workload is
  - why it is reasonable
  - how it will be used during testing

  ---

  # 2. Experimental Methodology

  Simplify this section.

  Keep only:

  - Test Environment
  - Baseline Test
  - Spike Test
  - Performance Metrics

  Remove sections such as:

  - Dataset
  - Hardware subsection
  - Software Versions subsection
  - Test Commands
  - Environment discussions
  - Network topology
  - Long implementation explanations

  A simple environment table is sufficient.

  Example:

  | Item | Value |
  |------|-------|
  | CPU | ... |
  | RAM | ... |
  | Operating System | ... |
  | Node.js | ... |
  | Apache JMeter | ... |
  | k6 | ... |

  Do not include shell commands or installation instructions. Those belong to the separate User Guide.

  ---

  # 3. General principles

  Remember that:

  - Tool Survey Proposal is a separate deliverable.
  - User Guide is a separate deliverable.
  - The report should summarize the methodology, not teach someone how to use the tools.

  Avoid writing like a research paper.

  Keep the writing concise and focused on supporting the seminar.

  Do not invent any experimental results.

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)
