      102
      103  ### 5.1 EShop Overview
      104
      105 -EShop is a small e-commerce web application built on a Node.js/Express backend with a SQLite database.
          - It exposes a REST API for authentication, product browsing and search, cart management, and checkout,
          - backed by a small seeded product catalog and two seeded user accounts (a regular user and an administ
          -rator).
      105 +EShop is a small e-commerce web application built on a Node.js/Express backend with a SQLite database.
          + It provides the core business functions of an online store: product browsing and search, user authent
          +ication, cart management, and checkout. These functions are exposed through a REST API.
      106
      107  ### 5.2 System Architecture
      108
      109 -- **Backend:** a single-process Express.js application.
      110 -- **Database:** SQLite, which serializes writes through a single connection. The schema is reset and r
          -e-seeded on every server restart, which provides a consistent starting state for each measurement roun
          -d but means the database is not durable across restarts.
      111 -- **Authentication:** JWT-based login. Issued tokens do not expire, which affects how long a session t
          -oken can remain valid and reusable across a load test.
      112 -- **State:** the shopping cart is held in server memory rather than the database; it resets on restart
          - and can grow unbounded across repeated test runs.
      113 -- **Rate limiting:** a global limiter caps each client IP address to 200 requests within a 15-minute w
          -indow, applied across all API routes. This is the single most important constraint for load testing ES
          -hop: at 50 or 500 virtual users, the limiter would be reached long before the system's real capacity,
          -invalidating any measurement unless it is disabled for the duration of the test. The backend provides
          -a dedicated testing configuration to disable the limiter during measurement runs.
      109 +- **Backend:** a single-process Express.js (Node.js) application.
      110 +- **Database:** SQLite, used to persist product, user, and order data.
      111 +- **Authentication:** session-based login using JWT tokens.
      112 +- **Rate limiting:** the API applies a request-rate limit per client IP address.
      113
      114  ### 5.3 Testing Scope
      115
      117 -The performance test targets four core flows, matching the workload model in Section 7:
      116 +The performance test targets four core user workflows:
      117
      119 -- **Browse** — retrieving the product listing.
      120 -- **Search** — a parameterized, keyword-based product search.
      118 +- **Browse** — viewing the product listing.
      119 +- **Search** — searching for products by keyword.
      120  - **Add to Cart** — adding a product to an authenticated user's cart.
      121  - **Checkout** — submitting an authenticated order.
      122
      124 -Two behavioral details are relevant to test design: a request for a non-existent product returns a suc
          -cessful (HTTP 200) response with an empty body rather than an error status, so assertions must check r
          -esponse content rather than status code alone; and the checkout flow accepts the order total supplied
          -by the client without independently recalculating it.
      123 +**Out of scope:** administrative endpoints, non-HTTP protocols, and browser/UI-level testing — this is
          + an API-level performance test.
      124
      126 -Out of scope: administrative endpoints, non-HTTP protocols, and browser/UI-level testing — this is an
          -API-level performance test.
      127 -
      128 -### 5.4 Test Environment
      129 -
      130 -[Add the hardware and host configuration used for baseline/spike execution; see Section 8.1.]
      131 -
      132 -### 5.5 Limitations of the Environment
      133 -
      134 -- SQLite's single-writer-connection model means database-layer contention, rather than the HTTP layer,
          - is the expected bottleneck under concurrent cart/checkout writes — a hypothesis evaluated in Section
          -13.
      135 -- Running the load generator on the same machine as the backend under test can introduce resource cont
          -ention that skews latency measurements upward — a threat to validity discussed in Section 15.
      136 -- The environment is local and single-instance; it does not represent a production deployment with loa
          -d balancing, connection pooling, or a multi-core/clustered process model.

      13  This report presents a performance testing study of **EShop**, a Node.js/SQLite e-commerce backend, car
          ried out as part of the T05 — Performance Testing seminar. The study defines a mixed workload model cov
          ering product browsing, search, cart, and checkout, implements that model independently in both Apache
          JMeter and k6, and evaluates the system under a baseline load (50 virtual users) and a spike load (50 t
          o 500 virtual users within 30 seconds). A large language model (LLM) was used to draft a "realistic sho
          pping" load script from a captured HTTP log, which the team then audited and corrected before use — an
          AI-assisted supplement to the two traditional tools, not a replacement for them.

      359 -### 15.1 Failure Modes
      327 +### 13.1 Failure Modes
      328
      329  The following failure modes describe ways the tool/system combination can mislead a tester, verified d
           irectly against the system's behavior rather than assumed:
      330
     ...
      333  3. **Silent search misroute.** An incorrectly formed search request can silently fall through to an un
           related route and return an empty, successful response, so a misconfigured script can report full succ
           ess while testing nothing. *Detection:* verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. *Mitigat
           ion:* validate the endpoint pattern and assert on result content.
      334  4. **Non-expiring session tokens.** Authentication tokens do not expire once issued, so a script relyi
           ng on mid-test token expiry will not observe it, and a long-running test may reuse the same token inde
           finitely — unrealistic compared to typical session behavior. *Detection:* inspect issued tokens for th
           e absence of an expiry claim. *Mitigation:* treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather
           than an inherited assumption.

  Keep:

  - Background

  Remove:

  - Objectives of Performance Testing

  The motivation is already sufficiently explained in the Background and Fundamentals.

  Keep only the essential concepts required to understand the rest of the report.

  Keep:

  - What is Performance Testing
  - Types of Performance Testing
  - Performance Metrics

  Remove:

  - Why Performance Testing
  - Performance Testing Process

  Reason:

  These sections are either repetitive or not referenced later in the report.

  Reduce this section to approximately half its current length.

  Do NOT repeat proposal-level discussion.

  - Spike Results
  - Result Analysis

  - response time
  - throughput
  - error rate
  - bottlenecks
  - observations

  Do not create a separate subsection for every metric.

      42
      43  ## 3. Introduction
      44
      45 -### 3.1 Background
      46 -
      45  Modern web applications must remain responsive and stable as user load grows. Functional testing verifi
          es that a system behaves correctly for a single user or a small number of requests, but it does not rev
          eal how a system behaves under many concurrent users, under a sudden traffic spike, or when a shared re
          source (such as a database connection) becomes a bottleneck. Performance testing addresses this gap by
          measuring response time, throughput, and error behavior under controlled, repeatable load conditions.
      46
      47  This seminar uses **EShop**, a Node.js/Express backend with a SQLite database, as the system under test
          . EShop is a small e-commerce application exposing REST endpoints for product browsing, search, cart ma
          nagement, and checkout, secured with a JWT-based login flow.
      48
      51 -### 3.2 Objectives of Performance Testing
      52 -
      53 -The general objectives of performance testing are to:
      54 -
      55 -- Measure response time distribution (not just an average) under realistic and elevated load.
      56 -- Determine the maximum load the system can sustain before response time or error rate degrades unaccep
         -tably.
      57 -- Observe how the system behaves during a sudden increase in traffic (spike) and whether it recovers.
      58 -- Identify the component that limits throughput (application server, database, network, etc.).
      59 -- Provide evidence-based input for capacity planning and Service Level Objectives (SLOs).
      60 -
      49  ---
      50
      51  ## 4. Performance Testing Fundamentals
     ...
      54
      55  Performance testing is a non-functional testing discipline that evaluates how a system behaves with res
          pect to responsiveness and stability under a given workload. Unlike functional testing, which asks "doe
          s the system produce the correct output," performance testing asks "how fast, how consistent, and how f
          ar can the system go before it degrades."
      56
      69 -### 4.2 Why Performance Testing?
      57 +### 4.2 Types of Performance Testing
      58
      71 -- **User experience:** slow response times directly reduce usability and, for e-commerce systems, conve
         -rsion rate.
      72 -- **Capacity planning:** performance tests reveal the load level at which infrastructure must scale.
      73 -- **Risk reduction:** traffic spikes (e.g., flash sales, marketing campaigns) are foreseeable events; t
         -esting for them in advance is cheaper than discovering the failure point in production.
      74 -- **Regression detection:** performance can degrade silently as code changes are introduced (e.g., an a
         -dded database query in a hot path); repeatable performance tests catch this the same way functional reg
         -ression tests catch logic bugs.
      75 -
      76 -### 4.3 Types of Performance Testing
      77 -
      59  | Type | Purpose |
      60  |---|---|
      61  | **Load testing** | Verify behavior under an expected, realistic concurrent user load (this seminar's
          "baseline" scenario). |
     ...
       66
       67  This seminar focuses on **load testing** (baseline) and **spike testing**. Soak, stress-to-failure, an
           d scalability testing are out of scope; see Section 13 (Discussion).
       68
       88 -### 4.4 Performance Metrics
       69 +### 4.3 Performance Metrics
       70
       71  - **Response time / latency**, typically reported as percentiles (p50, p95, p99) rather than an averag
           e, because averages hide tail latency that affects a meaningful fraction of users.
       72  - **Throughput**, the number of requests (or transactions) completed per unit time.
       73  - **Error rate**, the percentage of requests that fail (non-2xx status, timeout, or connection error).
       74  - **Resource utilization** (CPU, memory, I/O, database connections), when instrumentation is available
           , to help localize a bottleneck.
       75
       95 -### 4.5 Performance Testing Process
       96 -
       97 -A typical performance testing process follows these steps: (1) define objectives and SLOs, (2) model r
          -ealistic user workload, (3) prepare the test environment and data, (4) script and configure the test t
          -ool, (5) execute the test, (6) collect and analyze metrics, (7) report findings and recommendations. T
          -his report follows the same structure: Sections 5–7 cover the system under test, tool selection, and w
          -orkload modeling; Section 8 covers the experimental methodology; Section 9 describes the demonstration
          -; Section 10 covers the AI-assisted workflow; Section 11 covers the results; Section 14 covers conclus
          -ions and recommendations.

      228 -Reviewing the AI-drafted script against the real system surfaced a recurring set of mistake categories

      212 +Every AI-generated script was reviewed against the real system before use. The endpoint and authentica
          +tion fixes above were verified and applied directly. [Add remaining review evidence once finalized.]
      213
      230 -1. **Fabricated endpoint.** The draft referenced a search endpoint that does not exist in the system;
          -the correct endpoint uses a different path pattern. Corrected after comparison with the verified API.
      231 -2. **Missing authentication.** The draft omitted the login step required before cart and checkout requ
          -ests; a setup step was added to obtain and reuse a session token.
      232 -3. **Unrealistic action ratios.** The draft's fixed sequential flow does not reflect the browse-heavy,
          - checkout-light distribution used elsewhere in this study (Section 7.2); reconciling the two is an ope
          -n item.
      233 -4. **Think-time and pacing.** The draft required an explicit pause between actions to avoid back-to-ba
          -ck requests; pacing was set to 1–3 seconds.
      234 -5. **Hard-coded identifiers.** The draft referenced fixed product/user identifiers rather than paramet
          -erized values, a brittleness risk flagged during review.
      235 -6. **Weak assertions.** The draft's success checks needed tightening to assert an exact successful sta
          -tus rather than a looser range that would silently accept authentication or rate-limiting failures as
          -passing.

      268  ## 13. Discussion
     ...
      276  3. **Silent search misroute.** An incorrectly formed search request can silently fall through to an un
           related route and return an empty, successful response, so a misconfigured script can report full succ
           ess while testing nothing. *Detection:* verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. *Mitigat
           ion:* validate the endpoint pattern and assert on result content.
      277  4. **Non-expiring session tokens.** Authentication tokens do not expire once issued, so a script relyi
           ng on mid-test token expiry will not observe it, and a long-running test may reuse the same token inde
           finitely — unrealistic compared to typical session behavior. *Detection:* inspect issued tokens for th
           e absence of an expiry claim. *Mitigation:* treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather
           than an inherited assumption.
      278
      336 -[Add AI-specific limitations and common mistakes, building on Section 10.4.]
      279 +[Add AI-specific limitations and common mistakes, building on Section 10.3.]

           nt (no load balancing, connection pooling, or multi-instance clustering). The database's single-writer
           -connection model is a known architectural constraint (Section 5.2) that may dominate results in a way
            that would not generalize to a system built on a different database. The product catalog is small and
            fixed, and the traffic sample used for the AI-assisted scripting workflow (Section 10) is limited in
           size and partly synthetic rather than fully representative of real user behavior.

✻ Cogitated for 46s

❯ Tôi đã review lại toàn bộ. Theo tôi đây là phiên bản đầu tiên mà tôi nghĩ **có thể dừng refactor cấu trúc** và chuyển
  sang giai đoạn điền nội dung.

  Tuy nhiên vẫn còn khoảng **5 điểm** tôi sẽ chỉnh.

  ---

  # 1. Header của report (Nên sửa)

  Hiện tại:

  ```text
  Traditional tools: ...
  AI direction: ...
  ```

  Tôi vẫn giữ quan điểm nên bỏ.

  Header của một report chỉ nên có:

  ```text
  Course
  Topic
  System Under Test
  Group
  ```

  Việc chọn tool đã có hẳn Section 6.

  **Ưu tiên:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐☆

  ---

  # 2. Abstract (Nên sửa)

  Đoạn này:

  > Tool implementation details ... are outside the scope ...

  không thuộc Abstract.

  Abstract nên trả lời:

  * làm gì
  * dùng gì
  * kết quả gì

  Không nên nói report **không** chứa gì.

  **Ưu tiên:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐☆

  ---

  # 3. References (Nên sửa)

  Hiện tại

  ```text
  Books

  Papers

  Official Documentation

  Website
  ```

  Tôi thấy không cần.

  Một report seminar hoàn toàn có thể để:

  ```text
  ## References
  ```

  và đánh số theo IEEE hoặc APA.

  Đỡ phải suy nghĩ nguồn thuộc category nào.

  **Ưu tiên:** ⭐⭐⭐☆☆

  ---

  # 4. Placeholder (Nên sửa)

  Hiện tại đang trộn:

  ```text
  [Add...]

  [Summarize...]

  [State...]

  ```

  Tôi sẽ thống nhất thành một kiểu.

  Ví dụ:

  ```text
  [To be completed after the experiment.]
  ```

  hoặc

  ```text
  [TODO]
  ```

  Đọc chuyên nghiệp hơn.

  **Ưu tiên:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐☆

  ---

  # 5. Figure (Khuyến nghị)

  Hiện tại report toàn chữ.

  Tôi vẫn nghĩ nên có tối thiểu:

  ### Figure 1

  Overall Seminar Workflow

  ```text
  EShop

  ↓

  Workload Model

  ↓

  JMeter + k6

  ↓

  Baseline / Spike

  ↓

  Performance Metrics

  ↓

  Evaluation
  ```

  Một hình như vậy giúp người đọc nắm được toàn bộ seminar trong vài giây.

  Đây là **khuyến nghị**, không phải bắt buộc.

  **Ưu tiên:** ⭐⭐☆☆☆

  ---

  Tôi thấy các phần này đã ổn:

  ---

  # Có một điểm tôi muốn lưu ý

  Section 13.1:

  ```text
  Failure Modes
  ```

  Đây là nội dung vốn thuộc **User Guide** theo Stage S4.

  Nhưng tôi nghĩ **giữ lại trong report vẫn hợp lý**, vì ở đây nó không còn là hướng dẫn sử dụng tool nữa mà là
  **discussion về các tình huống có thể làm sai lệch kết quả performance testing**. Nếu viết theo góc nhìn phân tích
  thì không bị trùng với User Guide.

  ---

  # Kết luận

  Nếu là tôi, đây sẽ là **vòng refactor cuối cùng**.

  Sau đó tôi sẽ **đóng băng cấu trúc report** và chuyển sang:

  1. Điền kết quả thực nghiệm.
  2. Thêm biểu đồ/hình.
  3. Thêm tài liệu tham khảo.
  4. Chỉnh câu chữ cuối cùng.

  Nếu tiếp tục refactor nữa, lợi ích sẽ rất nhỏ nhưng nguy cơ làm mất tính ổn định của report sẽ tăng lên. Theo tôi,
  report hiện tại đã đủ tốt để bước sang giai đoạn hoàn thiện nội dung.

      287
      288  ## 13. Discussion
      289
      290 -### 13.1 Failure Modes
      290 +### 13.1 Limitations
      291
      292 -The following failure modes describe ways the tool/system combination can mislead a tester, verified d
          -irectly against the system's behavior rather than assumed:
      293 -
      294 -1. **Rate limiter masking real capacity.** The application-level rate limiter, if left enabled, is rea
          -ched long before the system's real capacity at 50 or 500 virtual users, producing a wall of rejected r
          -equests and an artificially capped result. *Detection:* a sharp, early rise in rejected requests. *Mit
          -igation:* disable the limiter for the duration of measurement (Section 5.2).
      295 -2. **False-positive success on invalid lookups.** A request for a non-existent product returns a succe
          -ssful response with an empty body rather than an error status. *Detection:* assertions must inspect re
          -sponse content, not status code alone. *Mitigation:* add content-based checks for product-detail reque
          -sts.
      296 -3. **Silent search misroute.** An incorrectly formed search request can silently fall through to an un
          -related route and return an empty, successful response, so a misconfigured script can report full succ
          -ess while testing nothing. *Detection:* verify non-trivial result counts in search responses. *Mitigat
          -ion:* validate the endpoint pattern and assert on result content.
      297 -4. **Non-expiring session tokens.** Authentication tokens do not expire once issued, so a script relyi
          -ng on mid-test token expiry will not observe it, and a long-running test may reuse the same token inde
          -finitely — unrealistic compared to typical session behavior. *Detection:* inspect issued tokens for th
          -e absence of an expiry claim. *Mitigation:* treat token lifetime as an explicit test parameter rather
          -than an inherited assumption.
      298 -
      299 -[Add AI-specific limitations and common mistakes, building on Section 10.3.]
      300 -
      301 -### 13.2 Limitations
      302 -
      292  Testing is performed on a local, single-machine setup rather than a representative production deployme
           nt (no load balancing, connection pooling, or multi-instance clustering). The database's single-writer
           -connection model is a known architectural constraint (Section 5.2) that may dominate results in a way
            that would not generalize to a system built on a different database. The product catalog is small and
            fixed, and the traffic sample used for the AI-assisted scripting workflow (Section 10) is limited in
           size and partly synthetic rather than fully representative of real user behavior.
      293
      294  This study also covers load and spike testing only (Section 4.2); soak/endurance testing, stress-to-fa
           ilure testing, and scalability testing across multiple configurations are out of scope. Resource utili
           zation monitoring may not be captured, which limits root-cause analysis to latency and error-rate evid
           ence alone.
      295
      307 -### 13.3 Threats to Validity
      296 +### 13.2 Threats to Validity
      297
      298  - Running the load generator on the same host as the system under test can inflate measured latency du
           e to shared CPU and network resources.
      299  - The workload mix (Section 7.1) is a reasoned assumption rather than a fully validated observation of
            real traffic.

❯ Tôi đánh giá tổng thể: **8.8/10**.

  Đây là một section tốt, logic và đọc khá chuyên nghiệp. Tuy nhiên, nếu đặt nó trong bối cảnh **seminar ở trường**
  thay vì **design document**, tôi thấy vẫn còn vài điểm cần chỉnh để nó tự nhiên hơn và bám requirement hơn.

  # Điểm mạnh

  ### 1. Định nghĩa đúng phạm vi

  Ngay câu đầu:

  > This section describes only the 10-minute Live Demo...

  Tôi rất thích.

  Nó giải quyết luôn việc người đọc thắc mắc tại sao không có Audience Activity.

  ---

  ### 2. Demo Objective

  Đủ tốt.

  Nó kết nối được

  Pitch
  ↓

  Demo
  ↓

  Activity

  => narrative đẹp.

  ---

  ### 3. Timeline

  Đây là phần mạnh nhất.

  Timeline rất rõ.

  10 phút hoàn toàn khả thi.

  ---

  ### 4. Learning Outcomes

  Đây là thứ trước đó report chưa có.

  Nó giúp section này có ý nghĩa hơn.

  ---

  # Những điểm tôi sẽ sửa

  ## 1. Demo đang hơi "tool-driven"

  Đây là điểm lớn nhất.

  Hiện tại flow là

  ```
  Objective

  ↓

  Workload

  ↓

  k6

  ↓

  JMeter

  ↓

  AI

  ↓

  Compare
  ```

  Trong khi seminar của bạn thực ra đang muốn dạy

  ```
  Performance Testing

  ↓

  Need workload

  ↓

  Implement workload

  ↓

  Run

  ↓

  Observe

  ↓

  Analyze
  ```

  Tức là tool chỉ là phương tiện.

  ---

  Ví dụ

  ```
  2:30

  Run k6

  4:30

  Run JMeter
  ```

  Đọc lên hơi giống

  > hôm nay mình demo tool.

  Trong khi seminar là

  > hôm nay mình demo Performance Testing.

  Tôi sẽ đổi wording để luôn nhấn mạnh:

  > demonstrate the baseline workload using k6

  thay vì

  > start k6

  Nghe khác hẳn.

  ---

  ## 2. AI đang hơi "đứng riêng"

  Hiện tại

  ```
  ...

  Spike

  ↓

  AI

  ↓

  Compare
  ```

  Tôi thấy hơi gượng.

  Tôi sẽ đổi thành

  ```
  Run

  ↓

  Show how the script was created

  ↓

  Human review

  ↓

  Explain why the executed script differs

  ↓

  Compare
  ```

  AI trở thành một phần của workflow.

  Không phải tiết mục riêng.

  ---

  ## 3. Demo Preparation

  Đây là chỗ tôi nghĩ Claude hơi over-engineering.

  Ví dụ

  > prompt prepared

  Không cần.

  Audience đâu quan tâm.

  ---

  Ví dụ

  > HAR available

  Cũng không cần.

  ---

  Theo tôi preparation nên là

  ```
  EShop running

  Scripts prepared

  AI-generated draft available

  Fallback results available
  ```

  Là đủ.

  ---

  ## 4. Có một timeline hơi kỳ

  ```
  4:30–6:00

  Open GUI

  Run non-GUI
  ```

  Thực tế...

  Nếu run non-GUI...

  Audience không nhìn thấy gì nhiều.

  Nếu đã demo GUI

  thì nên

  * mở GUI

  * giải thích structure

  * show kết quả đã chạy

  hoặc

  * chạy GUI

  ---

  Chứ

  GUI

  ↓

  non-GUI

  hơi khó hiểu.

  ---

  ## 5. K6 và JMeter

  Tôi sẽ đổi thứ tự.

  Hiện tại

  ```
  Run k6

  ↓

  Run JMeter
  ```

  Trong seminar

  đa số người chưa biết gì.

  Tôi nghĩ

  ```
  JMeter

  ↓

  k6
  ```

  sẽ hợp lý hơn.

  Vì

  GUI

  ↓

  code

  dễ hiểu hơn.

  Nếu ngược lại

  terminal trước

  GUI sau

  người mới hơi khó theo.

  Đây là **recommendation**, không phải requirement.

  ---

  # 6. Tôi sẽ thêm một transition

  Hiện tại cuối cùng là

  ```
  Hand off
  ```

  Tôi sẽ thêm

  > "Now that the audience has seen how a workload is executed, they will design one themselves in the Activity
  session."

  Rất nhỏ.

  Nhưng tạo cảm giác liền mạch.

  ---

  # Điều tôi muốn hỏi thêm (quan trọng)

  Có một điểm tôi đang phân vân hơn cả những góp ý trên.

  Trong timeline này:

  ```
  Baseline

  ↓

  Spike

  ↓

  AI

  ↓

  Compare
  ```

  **Bạn có thực sự định chạy live cả Baseline và Spike không?**

  Nếu đúng là live:

  * Baseline ~7 phút (theo report hiện tại: ramp 1 phút + hold 5 phút + ramp down 1 phút).
  * Spike cũng vài phút.

  => **Không thể nhét vào 10 phút**, trừ khi bạn dùng cấu hình demo khác hoặc chỉ chạy một phần rồi dùng kết quả đã
  chuẩn bị sẵn.

  Tôi nghĩ đây mới là điểm cần làm rõ nhất, vì nó ảnh hưởng trực tiếp đến tính khả thi của Demo Script. Nếu không xử
  lý, timeline hiện tại sẽ rất đẹp trên giấy nhưng khó thực hiện đúng trong buổi seminar.




  => Đây là review từ ChatGPT, hãy xem xét các proposal được đưa ra và update có chọn lọc.

● Tôi đã cập nhật Section 9 dựa trên review của ChatGPT, nhưng có chọn lọc — không áp dụng máy móc tất cả.

  Đã áp dụng:

  1. Wording bớt tool-driven — đổi "Start k6" / "Run JMeter" thành "Demonstrate the baseline workload using JMeter/k6".
  Key Message của từng row giờ nhấn vào workload/objective, không phải bản thân tool.
  2. AI tích hợp vào flow — gộp "show script → human review → why it differs" thành một beat duy nhất (6:30–8:00), thay
  vì để AI đứng tách biệt sau Spike.
  3. Demo Preparation gọn lại — bỏ "AI prompt prepared" và "HAR available" (đúng là audience không cần biết), giữ 4 ý:
  EShop running, scripts prepared, AI draft available, fallback results available.
  4. Sửa đoạn GUI → non-GUI gây khó hiểu — giờ rõ ràng: mở GUI để giải thích structure, sau đó show kết quả của lần chạy
  non-GUI đã hoàn tất trước đó — không còn kiểu "đang chạy GUI rồi chuyển sang chạy non-GUI live" nữa.
  5. Đổi thứ tự JMeter trước, k6 sau — theo đúng đề xuất, vì GUI dễ tiếp cận hơn cho audience chưa biết gì.
  6. Thêm câu transition — cuối 9.4 giờ có: "now that the audience has seen how a workload is executed, they will design
  one themselves."

  Về câu hỏi quan trọng (feasibility): Tôi đồng ý đây là điểm mấu chốt nhất, nên xử lý trực diện thay vì né tránh. Tôi
  đã thêm hẳn một đoạn trong 9.2 nói rõ: baseline (~7 phút) + spike (~3.5 phút) cho cả hai tool là không thể chạy trọn
  vẹn live trong 10 phút. Giải pháp chọn: baseline của cả hai tool được start trước khi segment bắt đầu và audience chỉ
  xem phần đang chạy/kết quả đã hoàn tất; chỉ spike được trigger live để lấy hiệu ứng hình ảnh (ramp lên 500 VU). Cách
  này giữ đúng workload model gốc ở Section 7 (không đổi VU/duration để "gọn cho vừa demo"), không bịa số liệu, và là kỹ
  thuật trình bày phổ biến trong live demo thực tế.

  Không áp dụng nguyên văn 100%: phần "AI feels standalone" tôi không tách thành 4 bước riêng như đề xuất (Run → Show
  creation → Human review → Explain difference → Compare) vì sẽ chiếm quá nhiều dòng trong timeline vốn đã eo hẹp 10
  phút — thay vào đó gộp 3 ý (creation, review, difference) vào một row duy nhất, vẫn thể hiện đúng tinh thần "AI là một
  bước trong workflow" mà không làm bảng dài thêm.

✻ Cogitated for 2m 19s

❯ Bản này tốt hơn bản trước khá nhiều. Tôi sẽ chấm khoảng **9.5/10**. Những vấn đề lớn đã được giải quyết:

  * Demo được giới hạn đúng trong 10 phút.
  * Không còn biến thành User Guide.
  * AI không còn là "màn trình diễn riêng" mà nằm trong workflow.
  * Đã xử lý được bài toán thời gian bằng cách giải thích baseline đã chạy trước.

  Tuy nhiên, vẫn còn một vài điểm tôi sẽ chỉnh để report "sạch" và nhất quán hơn.

  ---

  ## 1. Mâu thuẫn nhỏ giữa Objective và Timeline

  Ở Objective:

  > "...executed against EShop using two different tools..."

  Người đọc sẽ kỳ vọng cả hai tool đều được chạy live.

  Nhưng Timeline lại nói:

  > JMeter → show results from the completed non-GUI measurement run.

  Tức là thực chất:

  * JMeter: giải thích + show kết quả.
  * k6: chạy live.

  Điều này không sai, nhưng wording ở Objective nên phản ánh đúng.

  Tôi sẽ sửa thành:

  > "...demonstrates how the same workload is implemented and evaluated using Apache JMeter and k6..."

  Từ "implemented and evaluated" trung tính hơn "executed".

  ---

  ## 2. Demo Preparation đang hơi giải thích quá nhiều

  Đoạn này:

  > The full baseline (about 7 minutes)...

  Đây giống "ghi chú cho người viết report" hơn là nội dung report.

  Tôi sẽ rút gọn thành:

  > Because the complete baseline and spike scenarios exceed the available demonstration time, completed measurement
  results are prepared in advance as a fallback. The live demonstration focuses on illustrating the execution workflow
  rather than waiting for every scenario to finish.

  Ngắn hơn và mang tính học thuật hơn.

  ---

  ## 3. Timeline: JMeter

  Hiện tại:

  > open the GUI test plan to explain its structure, then show the results...

  Tôi sẽ thêm đúng một câu rất nhỏ:

  > "...to explain how the workload is represented."

  Vì trọng tâm seminar là workload chứ không phải GUI.

  ---

  ## 4. Timeline: k6

  Câu:

  > already running in the background since the start of this segment

  Hơi kỳ.

  Nếu người xem thấy terminal đang chạy thì OK.

  Nhưng nếu đọc report sẽ thắc mắc:

  "Khi nào bắt đầu?"

  Tôi sẽ đổi thành:

  > demonstrate the ongoing baseline execution in k6...

  Nghe tự nhiên hơn.

  ---

  ## 5. Spike

  Đây là điểm duy nhất tôi còn hơi lăn tăn.

  Hiện tại:

  > Trigger the spike scenario...

  Nhưng cuối cùng:

  > Place the JMeter and k6 summaries — both captured in full beforehand...

  Nếu spike chỉ mới ramp thì kết quả so sánh lại là kết quả chuẩn bị trước.

  Điều này thực tế hoàn toàn hợp lý.

  Nhưng nên nói rõ.

  Ví dụ:

  > Trigger the spike scenario to illustrate the rapid increase in load. The completed spike results will be presented
  immediately afterward.

  Rõ ràng hơn.

  ---

  ## 6. AI section

  Tôi rất thích câu:

  > the version actually executed differs after human review

  Đây là đúng tinh thần seminar.

  Tuy nhiên nên bỏ từ:

  > "realistic shopping"

  Vì đây là tên nội bộ.

  Đổi thành:

  > AI-generated performance test script

  là đủ.

  ---

  ## 7. Learning Outcomes

  Đoạn này gần như hoàn chỉnh.

  Chỉ có bullet cuối:

  > AI can accelerate first-draft...

  Tôi sẽ nối luôn với seminar:

  > AI can accelerate first-draft script generation, but engineering judgment remains essential before any generated
  script is used in performance testing.

  Nghe học thuật hơn.

  ---

  # Điều tôi thích nhất ở version này

  Đó là bạn đã xử lý được vấn đề mà hôm qua chúng ta còn mơ hồ:

  > **Live Demo không phải là chạy đủ mọi thứ từ đầu đến cuối.**

  Mà là:

  ```text
  Chuẩn bị trước
        ↓
  Trình diễn những phần có giá trị giáo dục
        ↓
  Dùng kết quả đã chuẩn bị để hoàn thành câu chuyện
  ```

  Đây là cách rất nhiều conference và workshop kỹ thuật thực hiện. Người trình bày không để khán giả chờ nhiều phút chỉ
  để xem một tiến trình chạy xong; họ dùng kết quả đã chuẩn bị sẵn khi điều đó giúp giữ nhịp của buổi trình bày.

  Nếu chỉ xét riêng Section 9, tôi nghĩ bản này đã đủ chất lượng để giữ lại. Những góp ý trên chủ yếu là tinh chỉnh câu
  chữ và làm rõ một vài chỗ có thể gây thắc mắc, chứ không cần thay đổi cấu trúc nữa.



  => Review các comment từ ChatGPT và update có chủ đích và chọn lọc.

❯ We are continuing the Software Testing seminar project.

  Read these files first:

  - @CLAUDE.md
  - @report/report.md

  Task:

  Refactor @report/report.md so that it becomes a high-quality report template which can later be completed using real
  experimental results.

  The goal is NOT to invent sample metrics.

  Instead, build reusable analysis templates that allow another team member to replace placeholders with real
  measurements after the experiments are finished.

  Important principle:

  Separate the report into three conceptual layers:

  1. Experimental evidence (real measurements)
  2. Interpretation of those measurements
  3. Final conclusions

  The report should already contain layers (2) and (3) as reusable templates, while layer (1) will be filled later
  using actual experimental results.

  Requirements:

  ========================
  1. DO NOT invent any numbers
  ========================

  Do NOT fabricate:

  - latency
  - throughput
  - error rate
  - CPU
  - memory
  - bottlenecks
  - SLO outcome
  - comparison results

  Leave placeholders instead.

  ========================
  2. Replace generic TODOs with structured placeholders
  ========================

  Avoid placeholders such as:

  [TODO: analyze results]

  Instead, provide reusable structures such as:

  Observed Metric:
  <BASELINE_P95_MS>

  Interpretation:
  Explain whether this value satisfies the SLO defined in Section 7.6.

  Discussion:
  Describe the impact on user experience if this value exceeds the target.

  Use descriptive placeholders like:

  <BASELINE_P50_MS>

  <BASELINE_P95_MS>

  <BASELINE_P99_MS>

  <BASELINE_THROUGHPUT>

  <BASELINE_ERROR_RATE>

  <SPIKE_P95_MS>

  <SPIKE_ERROR_RATE>

  <SYSTEM_BOTTLENECK>

  <SLO_STATUS>

  instead of fake values.

  ========================
  3. Build reusable analysis templates
  ========================

  For every analytical section (especially Sections 11, 12, 13 and 14), provide reusable writing structures.

  Example:

  Observed Result

  Interpretation

  Engineering Discussion

  Transition

  The interpretation should explain HOW to reason about the metric, without assuming whether the result is good or bad.

  Whenever different outcomes are possible, provide conditional guidance.

  Example:

  If the observed p95 latency satisfies the SLO,
  explain that the system maintained acceptable responsiveness.

  Otherwise,
  identify which workload caused the degradation and discuss the likely bottleneck.

  ========================
  4. Improve Section 11
  ========================

  Transform Experimental Results & Analysis into a report template.

  Each subsection should contain:

  - placeholders for measured values
  - interpretation template
  - engineering discussion template

  Do not write conclusions based on imaginary data.

  ========================
  5. Improve Section 12
  ========================

  Tool Evaluation should not contain empty TODOs.

  Instead, prepare evaluation templates such as:

  Observed strengths

  Evidence from Section 11

  Observed limitations

  Suitable use cases

  Final judgement

  All evidence should reference actual measurements that will be inserted later.

  ========================
  6. Improve Section 13
  ========================

  Separate:

  Observed limitations

  Threats to validity

  Potential causes

  Future improvements

  Only discuss concepts that are independent of the final measurements.

  ========================
  7. Improve Section 14
  ========================

  Prepare reusable conclusion templates.

  Instead of:

  [TODO]

  write templates like:

  Overall SLO outcome:

  <State whether the baseline and spike workloads satisfied the defined SLOs.>

  Primary bottleneck:

  <Describe the dominant bottleneck identified during testing.>

  Lessons learned:

  Future work:

  <List realistic extensions based on the completed experiments.>

  ========================
  8. Preserve report quality
  ========================

  Do not rewrite completed sections unless necessary.

  Do not change the report structure.

  Do not add new report sections.

  Do not invent experiment outcomes.

  The final report should read like a professional academic report whose analytical framework is already complete,
  requiring only the insertion of real measurements and small adjustments to the interpretation after the experiments
  are conducted.

❯ We are continuing the Software Testing seminar project.

  Read these files first:

  - @CLAUDE.md
  - @report/report.md

  Task:

  Update @report/report.md.

  The goal is NOT to prepare a report template with placeholders.

  Instead, create a complete "golden sample report" that will later be rewritten using the real experimental results.

  This report will NEVER be submitted directly.

  Its purpose is to become the reference writing style for future AI-assisted updates.

  ==================================================
  IMPORTANT WORKFLOW
  ==================================================

  The final workflow will be:

  1. This report contains one complete, internally consistent example of experimental results.

  2. After the real experiments are completed, another team member will provide the actual metrics to an AI.

  3. The AI will rewrite ONLY the result-dependent sections while preserving the writing style, structure, and
  reasoning demonstrated by this golden sample.

  Therefore, the report should teach the AI HOW to write, not HOW to use placeholders.

  ==================================================
  DO NOT CREATE PLACEHOLDERS
  ==================================================

  Do NOT replace the report with things like

  <BASELINE_P95>

  <ERROR_RATE>

  <SYSTEM_BOTTLENECK>

  or interpretation templates.

  Do NOT convert the report into a reusable template.

  ==================================================
  INSTEAD
  ==================================================

  Create one realistic experimental scenario.

  The scenario should be internally consistent and technically plausible for:

  EShop
  Node.js
  SQLite
  50 VU baseline
  50→500 VU spike

  You may reasonably assume, for example,

  - baseline satisfies the SLO
  - spike causes noticeable latency degradation
  - SQLite write contention becomes the primary bottleneck
  - recovery occurs after the spike

  The exact numbers do NOT need to be accurate.

  They only need to be:

  - internally consistent
  - believable
  - easy for another AI to rewrite later.

  ==================================================
  WRITE THE REPORT AS IF THESE RESULTS WERE REAL
  ==================================================

  Completely write:

  - Abstract
  - Section 11
  - Section 12
  - Section 14

  using the sample measurements.

  Write naturally.

  Do NOT explain that the numbers are examples inside the report.

  ==================================================
  WRITING STYLE
  ==================================================

  The report should demonstrate:

  - how to compare baseline vs spike
  - how to discuss SLO compliance
  - how to identify the primary bottleneck

      293 +Under the 50→500 VU spike, both tools recorded p95 latency roughly three times the 500 ms target and p
          +99 more than double the 1000 ms target — a clear SLO breach on response time in both tools. The error
          +rate, however, stayed under the 1% ceiling in both tools, meaning EShop absorbed the tenfold increase
          +in concurrent users without rejecting a meaningful share of requests; the system became slow rather th
          +an unreliable. Throughput rose from the baseline's ~24 req/s to only ~180 req/s, well short of a propo
          +rtional tenfold increase, because the longer average response time reduced how many iterations each vi
          +rtual user could complete per minute — a sign that the system, not the load generator, was the limitin
          +g factor.

          +cy or continued errors after load subsided, indicating momentary saturation rather than a persistent r
          +esource leak or a state that required a restart to clear.

      297  ### 11.3 Result Analysis
      298
      293 -[TODO: analyze response time and throughput trends between baseline and spike, error rate and any thre
          -shold breaches, the observed bottleneck (e.g., database contention under concurrent writes), whether t
          -he system recovered after the spike, and any other notable observations.]
      299 +Response time and throughput moved together from baseline to spike: p95 latency rose from roughly 210
          +ms to roughly 1,450 ms (about 7×) while throughput rose only from roughly 24.6 req/s to roughly 185 re
          +q/s (about 7.5×) against a 10× increase in virtual users — both figures point to the same conclusion,
          +that the system reached a hard capacity limit rather than degrading gracefully in proportion to load.
          +The error rate remained low throughout (under 0.7% at worst), so the degradation is a latency story, n
          +ot an availability story.
      300
      301 +**Identifying the bottleneck.** Breaking the spike results down by request type shows a clear asymmetr
          +y: browse and search requests (read-only) reached a p95 of only about 340 ms during the spike — a mode
          +st increase from their baseline figure — while add-to-cart and checkout requests (which issue writes)
          +reached a p95 of roughly 2,150 ms, an order of magnitude worse. This pattern is consistent with EShop'
          +s single SQLite writer connection (Section 5.2): as concurrent write requests queue behind one another
          +, their latency grows sharply while read requests, which do not contend for the same connection, remai
          +n comparatively fast. The rate limiter is not a plausible explanation, since it was disabled for the d
          +uration of the test and the observed errors were connection timeouts rather than HTTP 429 responses. C
          +PU on the single Node.js process approached full utilization of one core during the hold phase of the
          +spike, consistent with a single-threaded process waiting on serialized database writes rather than bei
          +ng starved of CPU across multiple cores. Taken together, the evidence points to **SQLite write content
          +ion on the checkout and cart paths** as the primary bottleneck.
      302 +
      303 +**Other observations.** The two tools' spike numbers track each other within about 5% (p95 differs by
          +60 ms, error rate by 0.1 percentage points), reinforcing that the degradation reflects EShop's behavio
          +r rather than a quirk of either load generator. No unexpected status codes were observed outside the e
          +xpected 200/401 range, and repeating the checkout-only subset of requests in isolation produced the sa
          +me pattern, ruling out a transient, one-off anomaly.
      304 +
      305 +**Transition.** These findings are used in Section 12 to compare k6 and JMeter on evidence rather than
          + reputation, and in Section 14 to state the overall SLO outcome and the primary bottleneck.

      355 +The workload model (Section 7.1) held up as designed: browse and search dominate ordinary traffic, but

      359 +Address the identified bottleneck directly: move checkout and cart writes off a single blocking SQLite
          + connection, for example by enabling write-ahead logging (WAL) mode or by introducing a bounded write
          +queue so that concurrent checkout requests are serialized predictably instead of blocking the connecti
          +on outright. Because the degradation was latency-based rather than error-based, consider whether a sli
          +ghtly relaxed p95 target under spike conditions specifically (as distinct from the steady-state SLO) w
          +ould better reflect an acceptable, temporary degradation versus a hard failure. Re-run the spike scena

  throughout, which is used as the basis for a fair, evidence-grounded tool comparison rather than a reputation-based
  one.

          -e in both tools was close to zero, consistent with a small number of transient connection resets rathe
          -r than any systematic failure. The two tools agree closely — p95 differs by only 10 ms and throughput
          -by 0.5 req/s — which gives confidence that the numbers reflect EShop's actual behavior at this load ra
          -ther than a tool-specific measurement artifact. At the expected steady-state load, EShop is responsive
          - and well within its defined SLO.
      279 +At 50 VU, both tools recorded p95 latency well under the 500 ms target, with roughly 290 ms of headroo

          +es confidence that the numbers reflect EShop's actual behavior at this load rather than a tool-specifi

          +ined SLO.

      293 -Under the 50→500 VU spike, both tools recorded p95 latency roughly three times the 500 ms target and p
          -99 more than double the 1000 ms target — a clear SLO breach on response time in both tools. The error
          -rate, however, stayed under the 1% ceiling in both tools, meaning EShop absorbed the tenfold increase
          -in concurrent users without rejecting a meaningful share of requests; the system became slow rather th
          -an unreliable. Throughput rose from the baseline's ~24 req/s to only ~180 req/s, well short of a propo
          -rtional tenfold increase, because the longer average response time reduced how many iterations each vi
          -rtual user could complete per minute — a sign that the system, not the load generator, was the limitin
          -g factor.

          +in concurrent users without rejecting a meaningful share of requests; the system became slow rather th
          +an unreliable.

      295 -**Recovery.** Once the spike's one-minute ramp-down back to 50 VU began, p95 latency returned to appro
          -ximately 240 ms — close to the original baseline figure — within about 90 seconds, and the error rate
          -returned to baseline levels over the same window. The system did not show any lingering elevated laten
          -cy or continued errors after load subsided, indicating momentary saturation rather than a persistent r
          -esource leak or a state that required a restart to clear.
      293 +**Recovery.** Once the spike's ramp-down back to 50 VU began (Section 7.5), p95 latency returned close

      295  ### 11.3 Result Analysis
      296
      299 -Response time and throughput moved together from baseline to spike: p95 latency rose from roughly 210
          -ms to roughly 1,450 ms (about 7×) while throughput rose only from roughly 24.6 req/s to roughly 185 re
          -q/s (about 7.5×) against a 10× increase in virtual users — both figures point to the same conclusion,
          -that the system reached a hard capacity limit rather than degrading gracefully in proportion to load.
          -The error rate remained low throughout (under 0.7% at worst), so the degradation is a latency story, n
          -ot an availability story.
      297 +Response time grew far faster than load did: p95 latency rose from roughly 210 ms at baseline to rough
          +ly 1,450 ms at spike — a roughly 7× increase — against a 10× increase in virtual users (50 → 500, Sect
          +ion 7.4–7.5). This disproportionate growth indicates the system was approaching a hard limit rather th
          +an degrading gracefully in step with load.
      298
      301 -**Identifying the bottleneck.** Breaking the spike results down by request type shows a clear asymmetr
          -y: browse and search requests (read-only) reached a p95 of only about 340 ms during the spike — a mode
          -st increase from their baseline figure — while add-to-cart and checkout requests (which issue writes)
          -reached a p95 of roughly 2,150 ms, an order of magnitude worse. This pattern is consistent with EShop'
          -s single SQLite writer connection (Section 5.2): as concurrent write requests queue behind one another
          -, their latency grows sharply while read requests, which do not contend for the same connection, remai
          -n comparatively fast. The rate limiter is not a plausible explanation, since it was disabled for the d
          -uration of the test and the observed errors were connection timeouts rather than HTTP 429 responses. C
          -PU on the single Node.js process approached full utilization of one core during the hold phase of the
          -spike, consistent with a single-threaded process waiting on serialized database writes rather than bei
          -ng starved of CPU across multiple cores. Taken together, the evidence points to **SQLite write content
          -ion on the checkout and cart paths** as the primary bottleneck.
      299 +**Identifying the bottleneck.** The error rate stayed low throughout the spike (under 1%), which rules
          + out the system rejecting requests outright; instead, requests were completing, just far more slowly.
          +Rising latency alongside a low error rate is consistent with requests queuing behind a serialized reso
          +urce rather than being turned away. EShop's architecture (Section 5.2) has exactly one resource of thi
          +s kind: SQLite's single writer connection, which every cart and checkout write must pass through one a
          +t a time. As concurrent write requests during the spike queue behind this single connection, the resul
          +ting wait time is the most plausible explanation for the latency growth observed in Sections 11.1–11.2
          +.
      300
      303 -**Other observations.** The two tools' spike numbers track each other within about 5% (p95 differs by
          -60 ms, error rate by 0.1 percentage points), reinforcing that the degradation reflects EShop's behavio
          -r rather than a quirk of either load generator. No unexpected status codes were observed outside the e
          -xpected 200/401 range, and repeating the checkout-only subset of requests in isolation produced the sa
          -me pattern, ruling out a transient, one-off anomaly.
      301 +**Other observations.** The two tools' spike numbers track each other closely (p95 differs by 60 ms, e
          +rror rate by 0.1 percentage points), reinforcing that the degradation reflects EShop's behavior rather
          + than a quirk of either load generator.
      302
      303  **Transition.** These findings are used in Section 12 to compare k6 and JMeter on evidence rather than
            reputation, and in Section 14 to state the overall SLO outcome and the primary bottleneck.

          + 9). Its built-in aggregate report generated clear percentile figures directly from the raw sample log
          +, with no extra processing needed to read off the numbers in Section 11.

          -eably more memory than k6 needed to reach the same 500 VU on the same host, and configuring the same p

          -mmand-line summary was also less immediately readable than JMeter's dashboard for a quick side-by-side
          - comparison, and had to be read alongside the exported JSON to double-check individual percentile valu
          -es.

      347
      348  **Overall SLO outcome:** EShop satisfied all three SLO targets under the baseline load (50 VU) in both
            k6 and JMeter, with p95 latency roughly 290 ms below the 500 ms target and an error rate near zero. U
           nder the spike load (50→500 VU), the system breached the latency SLO by a wide margin (p95 approximate
           ly 1.45 s against a 500 ms target) while the error-rate SLO was still met (below 1% in both tools). Th
           e overall verdict for this study is therefore a **partial SLO failure**: EShop is fit for its expected
            steady-state load but not yet fit for a sudden tenfold traffic surge.
      349
      350 -**Primary bottleneck:** Write contention on SQLite's single database connection, concentrated on the c
          -art and checkout paths. Read-only requests (browse, search) remained comparatively fast during the spi
          -ke, while write requests (add-to-cart, checkout) accounted for most of the latency increase, consisten
          -t with the single-writer-connection constraint noted in Section 5.2.
      350 +**Primary bottleneck:** Write contention on SQLite's single database connection. The spike showed late
          +ncy rising sharply while the error rate stayed low (Sections 11.2–11.3) — a pattern consistent with re
          +quests queuing behind a serialized resource rather than being rejected — and EShop's architecture has
          +exactly one resource of this kind: the single connection that every cart and checkout write must pass
          +through (Section 5.2).

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● All flagged evidence types have been removed from Sections 11, 12, 14, and the Abstract, with the surrounding
  reasoning rewritten to rely only on p50/p95/p99 latency, error rate, and SLO compliance (throughput was dropped
  throughout since it wasn't load-bearing for any conclusion once reframed). Section 13 already had no disqualified
  content from the prior pass, so it was left untouched, and Sections 1–10, 15 were not modified.

  Removed:
  - Throughput (both result tables, all supporting prose) — the "system reached a hard capacity limit" argument was
  reframed using p95 growth (7×) vs. VU growth (10×) instead.
  - Per-endpoint latency breakdown (browse/search vs. cart/checkout p95 numbers) — the bottleneck argument now reasons
  from the aggregate pattern instead: latency spikes sharply while error rate stays low, which is the signature of
  requests queuing behind a serialized resource, and SQLite's single writer connection (a static architectural fact from
  Section 5.2) is the only such resource in EShop.
  - CPU utilization claim ("approached full utilization of one core").
  - Connection-timeout vs. HTTP 429 discussion (used to rule out the rate limiter).
  - HTTP status-code range commentary and the checkout-only rerun claim (folded out of "Other observations").
  - Exact recovery duration ("within about 90 seconds") — now just "returned close to the baseline figure," in both the
  Abstract and Section 11.2.
  - JMeter JVM/memory-usage claim in Section 12.1 — replaced with a scripting-effort observation (parameterization and
  ramp/hold/ramp-down setup), which doesn't require any special instrumentation.
  - k6 JSON-export discussion in Section 12.2 — dropped, keeping only the CLI-vs-dashboard readability point.

  Consistency check: Section 14.1's "Primary bottleneck" and the Abstract were both rewritten to match the new
  architecture-based (not per-endpoint-measured) reasoning in Section 11.3, so the causal story is now told the same way
  everywhere it appears. Section 14.2–14.4 needed no changes — they were already grounded in latency/error-rate/SLO
  language plus design-level facts (workload weights, tool agreement, script corrections), none of which depended on the
  removed evidence types.

  The report still reads as a complete, internally consistent golden sample — same structure, same writing style, same
  partial-SLO-failure narrative — just with every claim now traceable to p50/p95/p99, error rate, and SLO pass/fail,
  which is what the T05 brief actually asks the team to capture.

  Task:

  Create a new file:

  @report/resources/Report_Update_Guide.md

  Purpose:

  This is an INTERNAL working document for Team 09.

  It is NOT part of the seminar deliverables.

  Its purpose is to help any team member update @report/report.md after the real experiments have been completed.

  The current report is intentionally written as a "golden sample".

  After the actual experiments are completed, another team member should be able to:

  - collect the experimental metrics
  - copy them into a standard format
  - give them to Claude
  - obtain a fully updated report while preserving the report structure and writing style

  The guide should make this workflow repeatable.

  ==================================================
  Structure
  ==================================================

  # Report Update Guide

  ## 1. Purpose

  Briefly explain:

  - why this guide exists
  - why the current report is written as a golden sample
  - why the report should be updated using AI instead of manual rewriting
  - that this document is an internal team resource

  --------------------------------------------------

  ## 2. Required Inputs

  Clearly specify everything that should be collected after the experiments.

  ### 2.1 Test Environment

  Include fields such as

  - CPU
  - RAM
  - Operating System
  - Node.js version
  - Apache JMeter version
  - k6 version

  ### 2.2 Baseline Results

  For both JMeter and k6 collect

  - p50
  - p95
  - p99
  - Error rate

  ### 2.3 Spike Results

  For both JMeter and k6 collect

  - p50
  - p95
  - p99
  - Error rate

  ### 2.4 Optional Engineering Notes

  Examples

  - observed bottleneck
  - recovery behaviour
  - unexpected observations
  - differences between JMeter and k6

  These observations should only be included if they were actually observed.

  --------------------------------------------------

  ## 3. Report Sections That Must Be Updated

  List every section that depends on experimental results.

  For each section explain exactly what should be updated.

  For example

  Section 1
  Abstract

  Update:
  - key outcomes
  - SLO summary
  - bottleneck summary

  Section 8
  Experimental Methodology

  Update:
  - test environment only

  Section 11

  Update:
  - metric tables
  - analysis

  Section 12

  Update:
  - tool evaluation
  - comparison

  Section 14

  Update:
  - summary
  - lessons learned
  - recommendations
  - future work

  Also explicitly list the sections that normally should NOT change.

  --------------------------------------------------

  ## 4. Standard Experimental Data Format

  Provide a copy-paste template.

  For example

  # Test Environment

  CPU:

  RAM:

  Operating System:

  Node.js:

  Apache JMeter:

  k6:

  # Baseline

  k6

  p50:

  p95:

  p99:

  Error rate:

  JMeter

  ...

  # Spike

  ...

  # Engineering Notes

  ...

  The template should be easy for another team member to fill in.

  --------------------------------------------------

  ## 5. Standard Claude Prompt

  Provide one reusable prompt that another team member can copy directly into Claude.

  The prompt should instruct Claude to:

  - read @report/report.md
  - update only the sections affected by the new measurements
  - preserve the report structure
  - preserve the writing style
  - preserve the reasoning style
  - avoid modifying unrelated sections
  - keep the report internally consistent

  The prompt should assume the user will paste the completed experimental data template underneath it.

  --------------------------------------------------

  ## 6. Verification Checklist

  Create a final checklist.

  Example

  □ Test environment updated

  □ Baseline table updated

  □ Spike table updated

  □ Abstract updated

  □ Tool evaluation updated

  □ Conclusion updated

  □ References unchanged

  □ Report structure unchanged

  ==================================================
  Writing Style
  ==================================================

  Keep the guide concise.

  Use clear headings.

  Write for internal team members.

  Avoid unnecessary explanations.

  The document should function as an operational playbook rather than a tutorial.

● Created report/resources/Report_Update_Guide.md. Summary:

  - Section 1 (Purpose) — explains the golden-sample rationale and why AI-driven updates beat manual rewriting for
  consistency.
  - Section 2 (Required Inputs) — exactly the four field groups you specified (Test Environment, Baseline, Spike,
  Optional Engineering Notes), with the "only include what was actually observed" guard on the notes.
  - Section 3 (Sections That Must Be Updated) — mapped against the actual current report.md content rather than guessed:
  Abstract (Key outcomes only), 8.1 (Test Environment table only), and full rewrites of 11/12/14. The "do NOT change"
  list explicitly covers 2–7, 9, 10, 15, plus a special note on 13 (Discussion) — it's usually unaffected since its
  content is environment/scope-level, not numbers-level, but flagged for a light re-check if the real setup differs from
  what's described (e.g., dedicated vs. shared host).
  - Section 4 (Data Format) — a plain copy-paste template matching the fields from Section 2.
  - Section 5 (Claude Prompt) — a reusable, copy-pasteable prompt that names the exact sections to touch/not touch,
  forbids inventing data beyond what's supplied, and tells the model what to do if Engineering Notes are blank (don't
  fabricate a bottleneck/comparison).
  - Section 6 (Checklist) — includes a concrete "no leftover sample numbers" check listing the actual golden-sample
  values (85 ms, 210 ms, 1,450 ms, 24.6, 0.6%, etc.) as grep targets, which is the most likely real failure mode of this
  workflow.
